# Chapter 1

## **CHAPTER 1**

Rain fell, but not on the city. It fell on the archives, on the ledgers, on the ink that spelled out what was taken. In the dry office, two men faced the accounting. One had the figures. The other had the ghosts. The space between their chairs was the width of an ocean, and just as deep.


Inside, the air was thick with the smell of wet wool, espresso, and the faint, sweet decay of old paper. It was a philosopher’s café, which meant it was populated by men who mistook obstinacy for profundity, and women who tolerated them. He saw her immediately. She was at the back, a parenthesis of stillness in the murmur. A notebook lay open before her, but she was not writing. She was watching the door, watching him. Her gaze was not an arrival, but a confirmation.

He navigated the narrow aisle, murmuring apologies to coat-backs. As he approached, she did not smile. Her face was a geography of intelligent endurance, pale skin stretched over fine bones, dark hair swept back and held with a simple tortoiseshell clip. She wore a charcoal grey sweater. No rings.

“Professor Valois,” he said.

“Monsieur Blanchard.” Her voice was low, textured like river stone. “Sit. You are precisely seven minutes late. The rain is no excuse. It is a permanent feature.”

He slid into the banquette opposite her, placing the satchel on the seat beside him. A waiter materialized. “Coffee,” Léopold said. “Black.”

“The same,” she said, without looking away from Léopold. When the waiter left, she said, “You have it.”

It was not a question. He nodded, patting the satchel. “It’s all there. The letters, the journal fragments, the… the final treatise. Everything my grandfather left in the strongbox. I had it translated from the Flemish. The handwriting was… difficult.”

“The handwriting of a man who no longer wishes to be read,” she said. Her eyes, a greyish-green, fixed on his. “You understand what you are asking? To convene this… symposium? It is not an academic exercise. It is a séance. We will be calling up ghosts, and ghosts are not known for their manners.”

Léopold leaned forward, his elbows on the small, sticky table. “He mentioned you. In a letter from 1960. ‘The only mind in that sterile faculty who might comprehend the geometry of a collapsing horizon.’ He said if anyone could finish the work, it was you.”

A flicker in her eyes, quickly banked. “Flattery from a ghost. A cheap currency. Your grandfather, Monsieur Blanchard, was a brilliant man who built a beautiful, terrible machine of thought. And then he spent thirty years trying to dismantle it, bolt by bolt, with his bare hands. He did not succeed. He left a blueprint of the wreckage.” She accepted the small cup of coffee from the waiter, held it between her palms. “Why now? Why you? You are an architect. You build things that stand up.”

Léopold looked out at the nebulous street. A tram clanged past, a smear of yellow in the grey. “I am renovating a warehouse in Antwerp. Last month, we pulled up the floorboards. Beneath them, we found bricks. Not ordinary bricks. They were stamped. Kongo. They were made there, shipped as ballast. The foundation of my beautiful, modern space is paved with colonial ballast.” He turned back to her. “I have been living in my grandfather’s house, going through his papers, for a year. I am walking on his floorboards. I need to know what is underneath.”

Professor Claire Valois sipped her coffee. She set the cup down with a precise click. “Very well. A séance. But we do not do it here. The air here is for tenure and petty grievances. We need a different atmosphere. A neutral territory.”

“Where?”

“The coast. The empty coast. Where the land ends and the conversation with the sea is the only one that matters. I have a house. Near Oostduinkerke. It is large, and it is cold, and it has a room that faces the North Sea. We will go there. You will bring the papers. I will bring the minds.”

“What minds?”

“A linguist from Ghent. A historian from Kinshasa. And a… a logician from Berlin. They are the only ones who might, collectively, trace the cracks in your grandfather’s machine. They are each, in their own way, exiles from their own disciplines. They will come. I have asked.”

“And what is the subject?” Léopold asked, though he had read the fragments, the frantic, looping script. He needed to hear her say it.

Claire Valois looked at him, and for the first time, he saw a kind of pity in her expression, the pity a surgeon might have for a patient who must finally be told the nature of the illness. “The subject, Monsieur Blanchard, is the crime that is also a foundation. The subject is the philosophical justification for theft, and what happens to the justifications when the theft is over. The subject is the ghost in the European machine. Your grandfather called it ‘The Aftermath Engine.’ We are going to attempt, not to build it, nor to destroy it, but to draw its silhouette. To see the shape of the hole it left in the world.”

She gathered her notebook, stood up. She left coins on the table. “Be at Brussels-South on Friday. The 8:15 train. Bring the papers, warm clothes, and no preconceptions. The coast will remove them from you.”

She walked out into the grey distribution of rain, and was gone. Léopold remained, his coffee cooling. He touched the satchel. The leather was smooth, worn. He thought of bricks stamped *Kongo*, holding up a polished concrete floor. He thought of an engine, not of metal, but of ideas, still running in an empty room. The bell sighed again as he left, stepping back into the damp. The mist adhered to him. It felt like the beginning of a tide.

***

The house was not a house; it was a bulwark. A squat, two-storey structure of weathered brick and defiant windows, it faced the North Sea across a width of scrub-grass and dunes. The sky was a vast, bruised plate, pressing down on the horizon where a thin line of silver suggested the division between water and air. The wind was a constant, articulate presence, speaking in the rustle of marram grass and the groan of the eaves.

Claire met them at the door of the station wagon, her hair whipping in the wind. “Welcome to the end of the land,” she said, her voice carrying easily over the gust. “The Wi-Fi is a myth. The silence is not.”

The linguist from Ghent emerged first: Pieter, a man in his fifties with a kind, rumpled face and eyes that seemed permanently focused on some internal etymology. He clutched a battered suitcase. “The train was full of… people,” he said, by way of greeting.

Next was the historian from Kinshasa: Kofi, tall and elegant in a dark wool coat, his gaze taking in the expanse of the coast with a measured, analytical calm. He carried a sleek laptop bag, an incongruous artifact. “The air smells different here,” he said. “It smells of forgotten battles.”

Last was the logician from Berlin: Elara. She was young, severe, her blonde hair pulled into a tight knot. She hefted a backpack, her movements economical. “The coordinates were precise. The environment is… suitably austere.” She looked at the house. “A good vacuum for thought.”

Claire led them inside. The interior was as Léopold had imagined: sparse, clean, dominated by books and the pervasive, salty damp. The main room was a library, with shelves rising to the ceiling. A large, scarred oak table stood in the centre, and at the far end, French doors led to a stone terrace overlooking the sea. A fire crackled in a large hearth, fighting a losing battle against the chill.

“Your rooms are upstairs. Basic. Functional,” Claire said. “We work here. We eat here. We do not entertain here. The entertainment is outside.” She gestured to the vast, moving grey beyond the glass. “That is our interlocutor. It asks very simple, very difficult questions.”

They dispersed to unpack. Léopold’s room was a white-walled cell with a narrow bed and a desk. He placed the satchel on the desk, its presence both a weight and a lodestone. From his window, he watched the sea, the waves arriving in relentless, foaming lines. They did not crash; they *insisted*.

Dinner was a simple stew, eaten at the long table. The firelight danced on their faces. An initial, polite silence settled, the kind that follows a group of strangers into a shared solitude.

It was Kofi who broke it. He spooned the stew thoughtfully, then said, “Professor Valois has been cryptic. She said we are to examine a ‘philosophical aftermath.’ I am a historian of the Belgian Congo. My mind lives in a specific aftermath. Is this connected, or are we speaking in abstractions?”

Claire looked at Léopold. “The papers are yours. You should begin.”

Léopold wiped his mouth. He felt their eyes on him—Pieter’s curious, Elara’s assessing, Kofi’s patiently insistent. He went to his satchel, returned with a slim, archival box. He placed it on the table, but did not open it.

“My grandfather,” he began, his voice sounding too loud in the room, “was Henri Blanchard. A philosopher. A phenomenologist. He was part of that post-war generation that tried to rebuild European thought from the ashes. He wrote about intentionality, consciousness, the structures of experience. He was… respected.” Léopold took a breath. The wind rattled the doors. “What almost no one knew was that from 1947 to 1950, he was an advisor to the colonial administration. Not in an office here. On the ground. In the Congo. He went as a young man, a thinker who believed European rationality could be a… a civilizing syntax imposed on the chaos of the world.”

Kofi’s expression did not change, but his stillness deepened.

“When he returned,” Léopold continued, “he never published the major work he was known to be preparing. He became reclusive. Eccentric. He left the university. The official story was a nervous breakdown. A failure of nerve.” Léopold tapped the box. “This is what he left. Not a finished work. It is a… a deconstruction. A thousand-page attempt to take apart his own earlier thought, brick by brick, using the tools he acquired there. He called it ‘*Le Moteur des Conséquences*’—‘The Aftermath Engine.’ It is not a memoir. It is not a confession. It is a philosophical autopsy. He is trying to locate the point where a beautiful idea—the sovereignty of the rational subject, the phenomenological reduction—becomes the justification for a crime. And not just any crime, but the crime that funds the very house of thought in which the philosopher sits.”

Elara leaned forward, her eyes sharp. “A meta-critique. He is attempting to log the logical fallacies inherent in applying a universalist framework to a particular, dominated subject. The performative contradiction of colonial phenomenology.”

Pieter frowned. “But language… the syntax he imposed. It would have been a violence in itself. To name the world for someone else is the first act of possession.”

Kofi finally spoke, his voice soft but carrying. “You have the letters, the journals from that time?”

“Yes.”

“And the… treatise? The autopsy?”

“Fragments. It’s chaotic. It circles. It’s full of gaps.”

Kofi nodded slowly. “So. We are not here to finish his work. We are here to map the gaps. The silence between his words is our primary text.” He looked at Claire. “This is the séance. We are to be mediums for the unspoken.”

Claire met his gaze. “Precisely. We are not here to forgive, to condemn, or to rehabilitate Henri Blanchard. We are here to follow the fault line he discovered in himself, and by extension, in the European project of reason. We are here to think in the ruin.”

A long silence followed, filled only by the fire and the distant, rhythmic insistence of the sea. Léopold opened the box. He did not take the papers out. He simply removed the lid, exposing the topmost page. It was covered in a dense, agitated script. At the top, a title was written, then heavily crossed out, then written again, as if the author could not decide whether to claim or disown it.

***De l’usage philosophique des ruines.***

*On the Philosophical Use of Ruins.*

Elara stood, walked to the French doors. She stared out at the dark. “A vacuum,” she murmured. “A controlled environment. We have the subject: the corrupted algorithm of colonial reason. We have the source code.” She glanced back at the box. “Fragmented, bug-ridden. Our task is not to run it, but to debug it. To see where it crashes.” She turned to face them, her back to the vast, murmuring dark. “We begin in the morning. We will need rules.”

“Rules?” Pieter asked.

“For the séance,” Elara said. “No idea is too sacred to be dismantled. No personal history is irrelevant. And we must speak in turns. The wind will have its say, but we must be orderly. Chaos is data, but we are not here to worship it.”

Claire allowed a thin smile. “The logician provides the ritual. Good. Tomorrow, we begin with the first journal. From 1947. The year of arrival.” She looked at Léopold. “You will read it aloud. Your voice, his words. We will listen not only to what he said, but to the space around it. The silence he brought with him. The silence he found.”

They cleared the plates. The fire burned lower. One by one, they retired to their white-walled cells, leaving the box on the table, its contents seeming to hum with a low, persistent frequency. Léopold was the last to go upstairs. He stood at the doors, looking out. He could see nothing now, only the reflection of the room—the table, the empty chairs, the dying fire—superimposed on the blackness. It was an image of a contained, temporary world, floating on an immense and patient dark. He touched the cold glass. The engine was here now, disassembled on the table. Tomorrow, they would begin to sort the pieces, and he feared what they would find, not about his grandfather, but about the house that his own mind, his own work, was built upon. The bricks beneath the floor. The tide outside, forever arriving, forever taking the land, grain by grain, away.

# Chapter 2

## **CHAPTER 2**

The light that came into the library the next morning was a cold, rinsed grey. It held no warmth, only clarity. It fell upon the oak table, now set with notepads, pencils, a carafe of water, and the archival box, its lid off. The sea was a sheet of hammered lead, silent from behind the glass.

Claire had arranged the chairs in a rough circle, away from the table’s head, denying anyone a position of authority. She sat with her back to the windows, her face in shadow. Pieter fidgeted with a pencil. Kofi was perfectly still, a cup of black tea cooling before him. Elara had drawn a small, precise diagram on her notepad—three intersecting circles labelled *Syntax*, *History*, *Logic*. The centre where they overlapped was blank.

Léopold’s mouth was dry. The journal from 1947 lay before him, its cover of faded green cloth blotched with moisture stains. He opened it. The first page was not text, but a sketch: a rough, hurried drawing of a geometric, modern building—a governor’s office or a railway station—imposed over a dense tangle of vegetation. The lines of the building were rigid, assertive. The jungle was a chaotic cloud of ink. They did not touch; they occupied the same space as two different dimensions, one failing to erase the other.

“Read,” Claire said, her voice quiet.

Léopold cleared his throat. His voice felt alien, a instrument he had not tested.

*“14 June, 1947. Arrived Léopoldville. The air is not empty. It is a substance, warm and thick as broth, and it carries smells I cannot name—vegetable decay, hot earth, something smoky and animal. One breathes not air, but the exhalation of the land itself. It is the opposite of the phenomenological reduction. Here, the world will not be bracketed. It presses in at every sense, a constant, noisy facticity.*”

He paused. The words, his grandfather’s words, hung in the quiet room. They were young words, brimming with intellectual vigour, a sense of challenge.

“He begins with the body,” Pieter said softly. “The assault on the senses. He is trying to establish his own presence as a perceiving subject, but the perception is already overwhelming. The syntax of his European body is inadequate.”

*“Met with officials at the Ministry. The discourse here is a curious alloy. Administrative French, crisp and logical, laid over the reality of the place like a grid on a living map. One speaks of ‘development,’ ‘productivity,’ ‘order,’ as if they are incantations that will reshape the matter. I asked about the native conception of time, of space. They smiled. A philosophical indulgence. Here, philosophy must be utilitarian, they say. It must serve the Engine.”*

“What Engine?” Elara asked sharply.

Léopold scanned ahead. “He doesn’t capitalize it again here. But later…” He flipped a few pages, his fingers careful on the brittle paper. “Here. *‘The District Commissioner speaks of the colony as ‘The Great Engine.’ Rubber, ivory, minerals go in one end; civilization, progress, profit come out the other. A sublime machine. I asked of the friction. He said friction is to be eliminated. Philosophy, he suggested, might be the oil.’*”

Kofi made a low sound, not quite a laugh. “The oil. To make the theft run smoothly. To quiet the squealing of conscience.” He looked at Léopold. “Continue. Read his reaction.”

Léopold found his place.

*“I am unsettled. Is my role to be the mechanic of this Engine? To provide the metaphysical justification for its gears? Husserl sought a ‘rigorous science’ of consciousness. Here, they seek a rigorous science of extraction. The structures of intentionality—consciousness being always ‘consciousness of’—what is the ‘of’ here? Is it the rubber tree, or is it the idea of the rubber tree as a unit of production? The native who taps it—is he a co-intending subject, or is he merely a biological component, a tool that perceives? My head aches with the humidity and with a dreadful, exhilarating ambiguity.”*

“Dreadful, exhilarating,” Claire echoed. “The addiction of the colonial mind. The horror and the thrill of absolute, unanswerable power. It presents itself as an intellectual problem, a phenomenological puzzle. It aestheticizes the atrocity.”

Elara tapped her diagram. “He identifies the core corruption: the reduction of a human subject to a tool that perceives. A logical category error with ethical consequences. But he is still entranced by the puzzle. The ‘Engine’ is an abstract concept. He does not yet see the blood in the oil.”

“Read a passage where he describes an actual interaction,” Kofi said. His voice was neutral, but his eyes were intent. “Not his thoughts. A meeting.”

Léopold turned pages. The entries became shorter, more agitated. He found one.

*“3 August. Visited a village with the agent territorial. The purpose: to explain new quotas. The agent spoke in a pidgin, a brutal simplification. The chief listened, his face a mask of polished ebony. He said nothing until the agent finished. Then he spoke, in a deep, melodic language. The interpreter, a boy sweating in a ill-fitting uniform, translated: ‘He asks if the white man’s God is hungry for rubber, that he requires so much of it.’ The agent laughed, a harsh bark. ‘Tell him his God is the Belgian state, and yes, it is hungry.’ I saw the chief’s eyes. They were not angry. They were… cognizant. He understood the metaphor perfectly. He understood he was speaking to a man who believed in a different, more literal kind of hunger. A failure of translation, not of language, but of cosmology. I tried to explain the concept of a social contract. The words felt like dust in my mouth.”*

The room was very still. The wind had picked up outside, a low moan in the chimney.

“The mask of polished ebony,” Pieter whispered. “He objectifies him even in the moment of recognizing his understanding. ‘Cognizant.’ Not ‘wise,’ not ‘resigned.’ A cognitive state. He is still analysing, even in the face of the other’s humanity.”

“The social contract,” Kofi said. The words were ice. “The ultimate European abstraction. A theoretical agreement between theoretical subjects. Here, the contract is written with one pen, enforced with another. He feels the dust in his mouth because he is speaking a spell that has no power here. The real language is the quota, the hunger.”

Claire leaned forward, her face coming into the light. Her expression was strained, focused. “This is the crack. Right here. The moment the beautiful abstraction—the social contract, the phenomenological subject—meets the reality of the imposed will. The tool that perceives looks back, and its gaze is not one of intentionality matching intentionality. It is a gaze that sees the Engine for what it is. And the philosopher, in that moment, feels his vocabulary turn to dust. This is the birthplace of his later silence. The beginning of the deconstruction.”

Elara was writing rapidly. “The logical progression is clear. First, the thrilling ambiguity. Then, the failed communication, exposing the violence of the simplified code—the pidgin. Then, the failure of his own high-level abstractions. The system is crashing. His next step should be to reject the premise of the Engine.”

Léopold turned the page. The next entry was a week later. The handwriting was larger, more erratic.

*“10 August. Cannot sleep. The night here is not an absence of light; it is a positive presence. A cacophony of insects, cries of animals I cannot identify. It is consciousness itself, raw and un-bracketed. I attended a ‘demonstration’ today. A village had failed its quota. The punishment was… administrative. Methodical. I stood and watched. I did not intervene. My role was to observe. To understand. To what end? To oil the Engine? I vomited afterwards, behind the official’s Land Rover. The agent clapped me on the back. ‘The first time is always hard,’ he said. ‘You get used to the smell.’ He was not speaking of the vomit.”*

Léopold stopped reading. His own hands were steady, but a cold nausea was rising in him, a sympathetic echo across the decades.

No one spoke for a full minute. The only sound was the wind and the faint crackle of the fire.

It was Pieter who broke the silence, his voice hushed with a kind of grief. “The language… ‘Administrative. Methodical.’ Even in horror, he uses the vocabulary of the system. He cannot escape its grammar. He vomits, a physical rejection, but his mind is still trapped in the syntax. ‘To observe. To understand.’ This is the ultimate perversion of the phenomenological gaze.”

Kofi had closed his eyes. When he opened them, he looked directly at Léopold. “He writes this. He records it. This is not a private diary of shame. This is field notes. He is still the philosopher, even here. The horror is a data point.”

“Or,” Claire said, her voice cutting through, “it is the first note of the deconstruction. He is recording the failure of his own philosophical detachment. The ‘observation’ has become complicity. The ‘understanding’ has become a sickness. He is documenting the poisoning of his own mind.”

Elara looked up from her notes. “The logical imperative now is clear: exit the system. Resign. Protest. But he does not. Why? The journal continues?”

Léopold nodded, turning pages. “For another two and a half years.”

“Then the addiction is stronger than the revulsion,” Elara stated. “The intellectual thrill of the ‘dreadful, exhilarating ambiguity’ is a feedback loop. He is hooked on the proximity to absolute power, even as he despises it. It is the ultimate test of his philosophy, and he cannot look away. The Engine is not just out there. It is in him.”

The observation landed with the force of a physical blow. Léopold felt it in his gut. He thought of the bricks, the foundation. Not just under the warehouse. Under the family name. Under his own cultivated, liberal sensibility.

“We should stop for today,” Claire said, though the grey light suggested it was not yet noon. “This is enough. We have located the crack. We have seen the Engine enter him. To go further now would be… indecent.”

But Kofi shook his head. “No. We have just begun. We have seen the European subject in crisis. We have not yet heard from the ‘tool that perceives.’ We have only his description of the mask, the cognizant eyes. That is not enough. We need the other side of the silence.”

“The journals don’t provide that,” Léopold said.

“No,” Kofi agreed. “They do not. So we must infer it. We must use what we have to triangulate the silence.” He turned to Claire. “You said we are mediums for the unspoken. Let us begin. Not with more of his words, but with the shape of the absence. What did that chief, whose name Blanchard never learned, what did he *see* when he looked at this young, vomiting philosopher?”

Claire held his gaze, then gave a single, slow nod. She looked at Pieter. “Linguist. From the description—the pidgin, the failed translation, the metaphor of the hungry God—what was the nature of the silence between them?”

Pieter took a deep breath, his kind face troubled. “It was a silence of untranslatable worlds. The chief’s question about God was not naive. It was a profound philosophical rebuttal. If your system requires this much rubber, it must be to feed something with a monstrous appetite. He is diagnosing the pathology of the Engine. Blanchard’s social contract is dust because the chief is speaking from a cosmology where relationships are reciprocal, where taking too much from the land or from people has spiritual consequences. Blanchard hears a metaphor. The chief is stating a literal, spiritual truth. The silence is the sound of two realities passing through each other without touching.”

Elara nodded, adding to her diagram. “Two incommensurable logical systems. The colonial system operates on a brute-force override. It does not seek to translate; it seeks to replace. Blanchard is caught in the middle, his own system failing to bridge the gap.”

Kofi looked out at the sea. “So what did the chief see? He saw a young white man, sickened, but still standing with the agent. He saw a man who possessed words like ‘social contract’ but who was powerless, or unwilling, to stop a methodical punishment. He saw not a philosopher, but a particular kind of ghost—a man whose spirit was already separated from his body by the Engine. A man who watched. The chief’s cognizance was the knowledge that this man, for all his education, was perhaps the most trapped of them all. A tool that perceives its own function, and is sickened by it, but does not stop.”

The words settled over Léopold. He saw his grandfather, not as a remote, tragic figure, but as a young man in the sun, vomit on his shoes, his beautiful mind a cage of failing abstractions. He saw the chief’s eyes, seeing that cage. The shame was not in the watching; it was in being *seen* watching, and understood.

Claire stood up, went to the French doors. She placed her palm against the cold glass. “The Aftermath Engine,” she said to the grey sea. “It is not a thing he built there. It is the thing that was built *in him*. A perpetual motion machine of guilt, horror, and intellectual fascination. He brought it home. He spent his life trying to diagram its workings. And we…” She turned back to them, her face pale. “We have just placed the first piece on the table. The piece marked ‘complicity.’ The game is not to solve it. The game is to see if we can look at it without becoming ghosts ourselves.”

She left the room, walking out onto the terrace into the wind. The others remained, surrounded by the ghost of the young man’s words, the shape of the chief’s silence, and the cold, clarifying light that showed everything and forgave nothing.

# Chapter 3

## **CHAPTER 3**

The wind stole Claire’s words the moment she stepped outside, whipping them away into the vast, grey noise. She stood on the terrace, a straight, dark figure against the moving backdrop of sea and sky, her arms wrapped around herself. Inside, the silence she left was dense, charged.

Pieter was meticulously cleaning his glasses with a handkerchief, a repetitive, soothing motion. Elara stared at her diagram, her pen now idle. Kofi’s gaze was fixed on the green journal, as if he could, through will alone, summon the counter-text from its pages.

Léopold felt a pressure in his chest, a constriction that was part shame, part a dreadful, sinking understanding. The séance was no longer an intellectual exercise. It was an excavation, and they had struck a septic layer. The smell was in the room.

“We should…” he began, but his voice failed. He tried again. “We should perhaps follow her lead. Take a pause.”

“A pause from what?” Kofi asked, not unkindly. He looked up. “From the truth? It does not pause. It waits. It has been waiting in that box for decades.” He gestured to the journal. “He paused. For two and a half more years, he paused his own moral revolution. He wrote notes. That is the disease we are studying: the ability of the thinking mind to suspend its own ethical conclusions in the name of further study.”

Elara tapped her pen on the blank centre of her circles. “It is a logical trap. To condemn the Engine, he must first fully understand it. To fully understand it, he must continue to participate in it, even as a observer. The observation perpetuates the thing observed. A perfect paradox. His consciousness becomes a parasite on the atrocity.”

“And his language,” Pieter said, placing his glasses back on his nose, “becomes the host. It sickens. You heard it. ‘Administrative, methodical.’ The vocabulary of bureaucracy used to describe a terror. This is how the Engine protects itself—it translates violence into procedure. Even his revulsion is filtered through the official lexicon. There is no word for what he saw in his philosophical dictionary. So he uses the words of the perpetrators, and in doing so, he grants them a terrible, neutral legitimacy.”

Léopold stood abruptly, the chair legs scraping on the floor. He needed air that wasn’t freighted with these thoughts. He walked to the French doors, but did not go out. He watched Claire, a sentinel facing the void. He understood now why she had chosen this place. There was no comfort here. No distraction. Only the elemental confrontation: the solid, stubborn land being slowly taken by the relentless sea. It was the perfect metaphor for their work—the slow, inevitable erosion of certainties.

“We need the counterpoint,” Léopold said, turning back. His voice was stronger now. “We have his descent. We need… not an answer, but the other voice in the dialogue. The one he couldn’t hear, or couldn’t record.”

Kofi nodded slowly. “My field is the archives of the oppressed. The lists of names, the tax records, the reports of rebellions. The silence there is different. It is a silence imposed by omission. But sometimes, in the margins of colonial reports, in the transcripts of trials, the voice breaks through. A statement, a question, a song. Fragments.” He looked at Léopold. “Do you have anything else? Letters from others? Official reports he kept?”

Léopold went to the box. Beneath the journals were folders of loose papers. He lifted them out, carried them to the table. “Mostly his own drafts. Some official correspondence. Pamphlets.” He sifted through. His fingers brushed against a different texture—a thicker, rougher paper. He pulled it out.

It was not a letter. It was a drawing, but not like the one in the journal. This was on a sheet of cheap, pulpy paper, now browned at the edges. It was a charcoal sketch. The style was not European. It was representational, but fluid, the lines economical and powerful. It depicted a tree. But the tree was also a machine; its roots were pistons, digging into the earth. Its branches were conveyor belts, and instead of fruit, they carried tiny, schematic human figures towards a gaping maw in the trunk that resembled the boiler of a steam engine. In the lower corner, a small, careful signature in Roman letters: *Luzolo*.

The group gathered around it. The image was stark, devastating in its simplicity.

“My God,” Pieter breathed.

“It’s the Engine,” Elara said, her analytical tone tinged with awe. “The native conception of it. Not an abstract metaphor. An organic-mechanical hybrid. A tree that consumes people. This is the hungry God.”

Kofi reached out, but did not touch the paper. His face was a mask of profound recognition. “Luzolo. It is a name. An artist. Or a prophet. This… this is the counter-text. This is the gaze looking back. Not with cognizant eyes, but with a visionary’s clarity. He saw the synthesis that Blanchard could only analyse: the fusion of the natural world and the colonial machine into a single, monstrous organism.”

“How did my grandfather get this?” Léopold whispered.

“Perhaps he asked for it,” Claire said. They hadn’t heard her come back in. Her cheeks were flushed from the wind. She stood at the edge of the group, looking at the drawing. “Perhaps he met this Luzolo. Perhaps, in a moment of desperate need for a translation that words could not provide, he asked, ‘How do you see it?’ And this was the answer.” She stepped closer. “This is not a fragment. This is a complete philosophical treatise. More complete than a thousand pages of Blanchard’s tortured prose. The tree is rooted. It is of the land. The machine is foreign, grafted onto it. And the whole creation is a carnivorous system. It consumes its own. This is the cosmology of the Engine, from the inside.”

The discovery changed the atmosphere in the room. The weight was still there, but it had shifted. It was no longer just the weight of European guilt; it was the heavier, more profound weight of another intelligence, another way of seeing, that had comprehended the horror in its totality and rendered it as art. Blanchard’s journals were the frantic scribbles of a man lost in the labyrinth. Luzolo’s drawing was a map of the labyrinth itself, drawn from above.

“We must find out who he was,” Kofi said, his historian’s instinct ignited. “Luzolo. There may be records. Trials, censuses. An artist with this vision would not have gone unnoticed by the authorities. He would have been seen as a threat.”

“The journals might mention him,” Léopold said, his hands already moving back to the 1947 volume. He began to scan, not reading fully, looking for the name. The entries after the “demonstration” were sparse, internal. Months passed in a blur of heat, illness, and philosophical doubt. Then, in early 1948, he found it.

*“15 March, 1948. Met with a remarkable man today. A carpenter, from a village near the railway line. His Christian name is Joseph, but he insists on his given name, Luzolo. ‘It means vision,’ he says. He speaks some French, learned from a missionary who taught him to read and draw. He showed me his sketches. They are not of birds or trees,



---

# Chapter 4

## **CHAPTER 4**

The discovery of the name was a key that did not fit a lock. It sat in the room with them, a tangible, silent presence, while the late afternoon bled into a bruised evening over the Parisian rooftops. The rain here was different from Brussels; it was a sentimental Parisian rain, falling in soft, regretful curtains that blurred the streetlamps into halos of tarnished gold. Inside the apartment, the air had grown thick with the residue of revelation—the sour tang of cold coffee, the dust motes dancing in the lamplight like agitated atoms of history.

Léopold’s finger rested on the journal entry. *“He showed me his sketches. They are not of birds or trees,”* and then, nothing. The page ended. The next leaf had been razored out, leaving a thin, cruel scar in the binding.

“A precaution,” Anaïs said, her voice low. She had not moved from her place by the drawing, as if standing guard over it. “Either Blanchard removed it, or someone else did later. To protect him. Or to erase him.”

Kofi was already at the small desk by the window, his laptop casting a blue pallor on his face. “Luzolo. Joseph Luzolo. Carpenter. Near the railway line.” His fingers flew over the keys, a staccato percussion against the quiet. “The colonial administration kept meticulous records. But they are records of extraction, of control. Not of vision.”

Léopold felt the familiar, administrative weight of the hunt settle upon him. It was a grey weight, like the Brussels mist. They had a name, but a name was a currency that often devalued the further back one went. He thought of the archives, those catacombs of filed despair, where lives were compressed into notations in a ledger: *‘Subject. Occupation. Disposition.’*

“We need context,” Léopold said, more to himself than the others. “Which railway? The Matadi-Kinshasa line? The Boma line? The year is 1948. The *Grands Travaux* were mostly complete, but the maintenance… it was a system of perpetual mending. A carpenter would be valuable.”

Anaïs turned from the drawing, her eyes finding his. In the dim light, they were the colour of dark slate. “He was not just a carpenter. He was an artist who saw the Engine. Blanchard found him, or was found by him. This meeting was not an accident.” She gestured to the mutilated journal. “Whatever was on that missing page, Blanchard could not bear to have it seen. Or could not risk it.”

Kofi let out a soft, frustrated breath. “The digital archives are a labyrinth of dead ends. Permission walls. I am hitting the administrative mist.” He leaned back, rubbing his eyes. “We need a physical record. A personnel file from the *Compagnie du Chemin de Fer du Congo*. A missionary school roster. Something paper.”

It was then that the doorbell rang.

The sound was not the tired sigh of a café bell, but a sharp, two-toned electronic chirp that was profoundly alien in the old building. It severed the atmosphere like a scalpel.

They froze, a tableau of conspirators caught in the lamplight. No one was expected. The few colleagues who knew of Anaïs’s work were not the type to visit unannounced; their communication was a slow, careful osmosis of encrypted emails and oblique references at academic conferences.

Léopold saw Anaïs’s posture change. The stillness she carried became something else—the poised tension of a gazelle that has caught the scent of a predator on the wind. She held up a hand, a clear command for silence, and moved to the intercom panel by the door. She did not speak into it. Instead, she pressed her ear against the grille.

A moment passed, measured in the frantic drumbeat of Léopold’s own pulse. Then, from the street below, the sound of a car door closing. Not the soft thump of a private vehicle, but the heavier, metallic clunk of something official.

Anaïs turned from the intercom. Her face was a mask of calm, but her eyes were electric. “There are two of them. In the street. They are not leaving.”

“Police?” Kofi whispered, already beginning to gather the journals.

“Not in that car,” Anaïs said. She crossed the room in quick, silent strides and killed the main lamp, plunging them into the gloom illuminated only by Kofi’s laptop screen and the faint glow from the street. “And police would have announced themselves. They are watching the door.”

Léopold’s mind, trained for decades on the containment of information, began to calculate with a cold, sudden clarity. The attack was not on their persons, but on their endeavour. It was a move to isolate, to confiscate, to return the knowledge to the shadows. He thought of the manuscript in his satchel in Brussels, a decoy, a ghost. Here, they had the heart of it: the drawing, the journals. They were tangible, and therefore vulnerable.

“The service entrance,” Anaïs said. “Through the basement, it leads to the courtyard of the next building. Rue de Valence.”

“We cannot take everything,” Kofi said, his voice tight.

“We take the drawing and the 1947 journal. The rest is replaceable, or it is known.” Anaïs’s decisions were surgical. She went to the table, her movements efficient and devoid of panic. She did not roll the drawing, but slid it carefully between two sheets of acid-free board from her supply, then into a slim portfolio. Léopold took the 1947 journal, its leather cover cool and supple under his fingers. He placed it in his own satchel, the weight a familiar and now terrifying comfort.

Kofi shut his laptop, plunging the room into near-darkness. “Who are they?”

“The inheritors,” Anaïs said, her voice barely a whisper in the dark. “The ones who believe some labyrinths should remain unmapped.”

They moved through the apartment like ghosts. Anaïs led them to a narrow door in the kitchen, disguised as a pantry cupboard. It opened onto a steep, winding staircase that smelled of damp stone and old electricity. They descended single file, the only sound their hurried breaths and the scuff of shoes on worn stone steps. At the bottom, a low corridor lit by a single, wire-caged bulb led to a heavy metal door.

Anaïs produced a key, the turning of the lock echoing like a gunshot in the confined space. The door opened onto a small, asphalted courtyard, hemmed in by high walls. The rain fell here in a steady, quiet drizzle. Across the courtyard was another door, green paint peeling like sunburnt skin.

“This one is never locked,” Anaïs murmured. “It leads to the lobby of Number 42. From there, we can go out onto Rue de Valence.”

They crossed the courtyard, the rain immediately spotting the portfolio in Anaïs’s arms. Léopold felt a profound sense of dislocation. Ten minutes ago, they had been immersed in the intellectual archaeology of a horror a century old and an ocean away. Now, they were fugitives in a Parisian rain, fleeing a threat with a very contemporary, very European silence.

The door to Number 42 opened as promised. The lobby was a dim, tiled space with a bank of mailboxes and the smell of boiled cabbage. They paused, listening. No sound from the street.

“Where do we go?” Kofi asked. The historian was out of his depth, his domain the ordered past, not the chaotic present.

Anaïs looked at Léopold. It was not a demand, but a genuine question. In that moment, the hierarchy of their little cell dissolved. She was the visionary, Kofi the archivist. Léopold, he realized, was the tactician. The administrator of flight.

“Not a hotel. Not a place that requires identification,” Léopold said, his mind sorting through possibilities with bureaucratic speed. “The Gare de Lyon. The all-night café in the *hall*. It is a nexus of transience. People come and go at all hours. We can think there. We can decide.”

Anaïs nodded. “And then?”

“And then we find the paper trail Kofi needs. But not from here. The attack means they have compromised your known channels. Your sources.” He met her gaze. “We must go to Brussels. My archives. My sources are different. And I have… a space. It is not comfortable, but it is unknown.”

The acceptance in her eyes was immediate. There was no time for debate. They pushed out onto Rue de Valence, turning away from the direction of Anaïs’s apartment. They walked quickly, but not at a run, three figures hunched against the rain, clinging to their packages of stolen history.

*

The café in the Gare de Lyon was a study in exhausted transit. The air was a soupy mixture of diesel fumes from the train sheds, cheap perfume, and the greasy scent of frying food. The light was a relentless, fluorescent glare that bleached the colour from everything, turning the night outside into a black void beyond the glass. They took a booth in a corner, its vinyl seats cracked and mended with silver tape. Léopold ordered three coffees from a waiter whose face was a monument to profound indifference.

For a long while, they simply sat, letting the adrenaline leach away, replaced by a deep, trembling fatigue. The portfolio lay on the table between them like a sacred relic. Léopold’s satchel, with the journal, was wedged securely between his hip and the wall.

“They knew,” Kofi said finally, staring into his small, black coffee. “They knew we had it. Or they knew we were close. How?”

“Surveillance,” Anaïs said. Her hands were wrapped around her cup, but she did not drink. “Electronic. Or human. My apartment was a logical place to look for me. My interest in Blanchard is not a secret. My request for the journal delivery from the library… it would have been noted.”

“By whom?” Léopold asked. “The library is a public institution.”

“With private interests,” she replied. “There are still families, corporations, whose wealth is rooted in the *Grands Travaux*. Their history is not a subject for academic study; it is a living, sensitive nerve. A drawing like this…” She glanced at the portfolio. “It is a visual indictment. It makes the abstract, concrete. It gives a face, a vision, to the consumed. That is dangerous to the myth of the benevolent engine of progress.”

Léopold understood. He had spent his career navigating the quiet, powerful currents that flowed beneath the placid surface of institutional Europe. It was an ecosystem of whispers, of filed recommendations, of gentle pressures applied in wood-panelled rooms. This, however, was not a gentle pressure. This was a direct, physical intervention. It suggested fear. It suggested that Luzolo’s vision, and their pursuit of it, struck closer to the present than he had imagined.

“We cannot stay in Paris,” Léopold said, giving voice to the conclusion they had all reached. “The train to Brussels leaves in two hours. We can be on it.”

“And go where?” Kofi asked. “Your apartment?”

“No. A place I keep. An old notary’s office, near the Marolles. My grandfather’s. It has not been used in years, but it is maintained. The building is… unremarkable. There is a strong door, and no one knows of my connection to it.” He did not add that it was where he sometimes went to read manuscripts that did not officially exist, a sanctuary from the administrative world he inhabited. It was his own private archive.

Anaïs studied him. In the harsh light, he could see the fine lines of strain around her eyes, the faint smudges of fatigue. But her gaze was clear, assessing. “You have been preparing for this,” she said, not as an accusation, but an observation.

“I have been preparing for the necessity of quiet,” he corrected. “This is merely an application of the principle.”

A faint, almost imperceptible smile touched her lips. “An administrator to the last.”

The shared trial of the flight had forged a new bond between them. The earlier formality, the dance of academic courtesy, had been burned away. They were now accomplices. The vulnerability was in their shared dishevelment, in the way Kofi’s hands still trembled slightly as he lifted his cup, in the protective curve of Anaïs’s body around the portfolio. Léopold felt it too—a stripping away of his own careful layers. The satchel against his hip was no longer just a professional tool; it was a lifeline, and these two people were the only others on the raft.

“We should look at the journal,” Anaïs said softly. “Before we move again. There may be something in the surrounding pages. A clue we missed in the rush.”

Léopold nodded. He slid the satchel onto the table, unbuttoned the flap, and withdrew the leather-bound volume. It felt heavier now, charged. He opened it carefully to the pages just before the razored cut. The entry from March 15th, 1948, was brief. He read it aloud, his voice low, barely audible over the din of the station café.

*“15 March, 1948. Met with a remarkable man today. A carpenter, from a village near the railway line. His Christian name is Joseph, but he insists on his given name, Luzolo. ‘It means vision,’ he says. He speaks some French, learned from a missionary who taught him to read and draw. He showed me his sketches. They are not of birds or trees, but of the machine in the forest. He sees it not as a thing of parts, but as a beast with a metabolism. He calls it ‘the eater of days.’ I asked him how one fights such a beast. He did not answer. He only smiled and said, ‘One does not fight the river. One learns the river. Then one finds the stone that changes its course.’ I have given him paper, pencils. I fear for him. His vision is too clear. Clarity is a death sentence here.”*

Léopold stopped reading. The words hung in the greasy air. *The eater of days.* It was a phrase of devastating, poetic precision. It named the Engine’s true consumption: not just of timber and ore, but of lives, of futures, of time itself.

“And then the page is gone,” Kofi said, his historian’s soul aching at the void.

Léopold turned the page. The next entry was dated two weeks later.

*“30 March, 1948. The silence from Luzolo’s village is absolute. I sent a boy with a message, a request for another meeting. The boy returned, wide-eyed with a fear he would not name. He said only that the carpenter was not there. The *chef de poste* has made inquiries of his own, he said. A routine matter. I went to the administration building myself, a foolish risk. I requested nothing, only observed. In the yard, being loaded onto a truck for incineration, were several crates of ‘confiscated native art and subversive materials.’ Among the splintered wood and torn cloth, I saw a flash of white. A corner of a page. It was the weight of my own paper. I am certain of it. I have become an accomplice to the erasure of a vision. I have given him the tools of his own destruction. My philosophy is ash. It cannot comprehend this. It cannot even bear witness. Only his art could do that, and I have delivered it into their hands. Unless… unless he kept something back. He was a man who understood the river. He would have a stone. He must.”*

Léopold looked up. Anaïs’s eyes were closed, as if in pain. “He kept it back,” she whispered. “The drawing we have. It is the stone. Blanchard never saw this. Luzolo completed it after their meeting. He hid it, or sent it away. And Blanchard, in his guilt, in his desperation to preserve this one fragment of truth, concealed it within his own journal, the one place they might not look. The personal, philosophical ramblings of a European. They would confiscate the ‘native art.’ They would not bother with the madman’s diaries.”

It made a terrible sense. The drawing was not just a map of the labyrinth; it was a message in a bottle, cast onto the dark sea of Blanchard’s own guilt, hoping to wash up on a distant, future shore.

“We need to find the river,” Kofi said, his voice firming with purpose. “The specific river. The specific railway line. If we can locate the village, the missionary school, we might find a record of him. A baptism, a census note. Something that proves Joseph Luzolo existed beyond Blanchard’s journal and this drawing.”

“The railway line near his village,” Léopold mused, tracing a finger on the sticky tabletop as if drawing a map. “Blanchard was stationed near Boma, but his work took him along the supply routes. The journal mentions the Matadi-Kinshasa line frequently. It was the primary artery. The ‘eater of days’ would be most voracious there.”

“Then that is where we start,” Anaïs said. “In Brussels. We find the maps, the company records, the missionary reports. We find the river.”

They lapsed into silence, a planning silence now, the shock metabolized into resolve. The waiter refilled their coffees with a disinterested slosh. The station announcer’s voice echoed through the hall, a sonorous litany of destinations: Dijon, Lyon, Marseille. Places of no consequence to them.

When the time came, they boarded the train to Brussels as the last of the night deepened towards morning. They took a compartment, sliding the door shut on the world. The rhythmic clatter of the wheels on the tracks was a soothing, mechanical mantra. Anaïs placed the portfolio on the seat between herself and the window. Kofi, exhausted, leaned his head against the glass and was almost instantly asleep, his breath fogging the cool pane.

Léopold sat opposite Anaïs. In the dim compartment light, her face was all shadows and angles. She was looking out at the black countryside streaking past, but he could tell she was not seeing it.

“Thank you,” she said, without turning. “For your… administration of flight.”

“It was the only logical course,” he replied.

“It was not,” she said, finally looking at him. “The logical course for you would have been to distance yourself. To return to your archives and your quiet life. This is not your fight. Not in the same way it is mine, or Kofi’s.”

Léopold considered this. He thought of his quiet apartment, his ordered shelves, the respectful silence of the reading rooms. He thought of the manuscript in his satchel in Brussels, a story of horror that he had helped to keep contained. A necessary containment, he had always believed. To prevent the past from poisoning the present. But Luzolo’s drawing had changed the equation. It was not a poison; it was an antidote. A truth so stark it could cauterize the wound of forgetting.

“I believe I have been administering the wrong thing,” he said, the words feeling strange and true in his mouth. “I have been administering silence. Perhaps it is time to administer a different kind of record.”

Her gaze held his, and in it, he saw not gratitude, but recognition. A shared understanding of the threshold they were crossing. They were no longer just researchers. They were curators of a dangerous memory.

*

The notary’s office in the Marolles was as Léopold had described: unremarkable. It was on the second floor of a narrow, 19th-century building sandwiched between a butcher and a shop selling discount fabrics. The staircase smelled of floor polish and old cabbage. The door to the office was heavy oak, with a frosted glass pane on which the words *“Maître L. Vandenberg – Notaire”* were still faintly visible in flaking gold leaf.

Inside was a time capsule. A large, dark desk. Glass-fronted bookcases filled with crumbling legal codes. A green-shaded brass lamp. A map of Belgium on the wall, its colours faded to pastel. It was cold, and dust motes swirled in the thin morning light that filtered through the slats of the blinds. But it was dry, and it was silent, and the lock on the door was formidable.

For two days, it became their monastery. Kofi set up his laptop at the desk, connecting to a secure network through a series of complex, anonymizing relays Léopold provided from a small, nondescript black box. He burrowed into the digital archives of the Belgian colonial ministry, the records of the *Compagnie du Chemin de Fer du Congo*, the missionary societies of the period. He worked with a focused, relentless energy, cross-referencing dates, locations, names.

Anaïs taped a large sheet of drawing paper to one wall. She began to create a visual timeline, not of events, but of vision. In the centre, she pinned a photocopy of Luzolo’s drawing. Radiating out from it were lines connecting to Blanchard’s journal entries, to historical facts about the railway construction, to photographs of the machinery used. She was mapping the context of the prophecy.

Léopold’s role was that of a quartermaster and a archivist. He procured food, coffee, blankets. He used his own deep knowledge of Brussels’s obscure holdings to guide Kofi. “Try the Jesuit archives in Leuven,” he would say. “Their cataloguing system is idiosyncratic, but they kept detailed reports from the field. Look for mentions of ‘artistic talent’ or ‘catechetical drawing’—they often noted such things.”

The atmosphere was one of intense, collaborative focus. The shared space, the shared purpose, the shared danger, welded them together. They spoke in shorthand. They brought each other coffee without asking. They took turns sleeping on the old leather couch in the corner, wrapped in the coarse wool blankets Léopold had found in a trunk.

On the morning of the third day, Kofi made a sound—a sharp, inhaled hiss of triumph.

“Here,” he said, his voice hoarse from disuse and coffee. “The *Société des Missionnaires d’Afrique*. The White Fathers. Station at Kimuenza. It’s a mission station near the Matadi-Kinshasa line, about halfway. Their annual report, 1935.” He turned the screen so Léopold and Anaïs could see. “A list of pupils showing ‘particular promise.’ *Joseph Luzolo, of Mvuzzi village. Shows exceptional skill in draughtsmanship and geometrical drawing. Has assisted in the drawing of plans for the new chapel school. A quiet, perceptive boy.*”

“Mvuzzi village,” Anaïs repeated, going to her wall and writing the name on the timeline. She drew a line from it to the Matadi-Kinshasa line on a map she had sketched. “Near the railway. A carpenter’s son would be valuable.”

“Now, the railway company,” Kofi said, his fingers flying. “Personnel records for local hires, maintenance crews… 1947, 1948…” He scrolled, his eyes scanning. “There. *Luzolo, Joseph. Listed as ‘menuiserie’ – carpentry. Attached to the maintenance depot at Kimuenza Junction. Employed: April 1946. Terminated: March 1948.*”

“Terminated the same month Blanchard met him,” Léopold said.

“The reason given?” Anaïs asked, her back still to them as she stared at the map.

Kofi squinted at the scanned, faded form. “It is stamped. *‘Raison: Indisponible.’* Unavailable. A wonderfully bureaucratic euphemism.”

“He was taken,” Anaïs said flatly.

“Or he vanished,” Léopold offered. “He understood the river. He may have seen the truck coming for his art and simply melted into the forest.”

“With his ‘stone’,” Anaïs said, turning. Her eyes were alight. “He had already drawn it. He knew what was coming. He gave Blanchard the other sketches, perhaps as a decoy, perhaps as a test. He kept the final vision. And then he disappeared. But he left the stone for Blanchard to find. He trusted a European, a man complicit in the system, to be so haunted by his own guilt that he would preserve it.”

It was a theory, elegant and hopeful. It painted Luzolo not as a victim, but as a strategist of resistance. A man who used the very tools of his oppression—literacy, drawing, the European’s own paper—to craft a weapon of truth.

“We need to know what happened to him,” Kofi said. “For the record. We cannot let him be another ‘indisponible.’”

“The colonial security services,” Léopold said, the words tasting of acid. “The *Sûreté*. They would have a file. Any native showing ‘subversive’ tendencies, especially one connected to a European intellectual like Blanchard, would have been noted.”

“Those files,” Kofi said, “are the most tightly held of all. They are not in the digital archives. If they exist, they are in a basement in the Ministry of Justice, or they were destroyed.”

“Or,” Léopold said slowly, an old memory stirring, “they were partially duplicated. For administrative cross-reference. The notary who handled my grandfather’s estate… he also handled the estates of several former colonial administrators. Men who had… souvenirs. Unofficial papers. He once told me, in confidence, that the most sensitive colonial records were sometimes copied in triplicate. One for Brussels, one for Boma, and one for the local *Chef de Sûreté*’s personal files. As insurance, or as leverage.”

“And when these men returned to Belgium,” Anaïs breathed, “they might have brought their ‘insurance’ with them.”

“It is a possibility,” Léopold conceded. “A thin one. Those personal papers would be scattered, lost, thrown out by heirs, or locked in attics.”

“Then we find the heirs,” Kofi said, a new fervour in his voice. “We find the administrators who served at Kimuenza or Boma in 1948. We trace their families.”

It was a monumental task. A needle in a continent-sized haystack. But it was a direction. A thread to pull. They began to compile a list, using Kofi’s historical databases and Léopold’s knowledge of the Belgian elite. Names emerged from the past: *Dumont, Verhoeven, Claes*. Men who had been middle-ranking officers in the colonial apparatus, now likely dead.

As the afternoon of the third day waned, Léopold made a decision. “I must go to my apartment,” he said. “I have been absent too long. It may be watched, but I need to retrieve things. Clothes. My own research notes. And the manuscript of Blanchard’s journals. It is the only other copy. We should have it here.”

Anaïs looked concerned. “It is a risk.”

“A calculated one. I will go after dark. Use the service entrance. I know the building.”

“I’ll come with you,” Kofi said, standing.

“No. Two is more conspicuous than one. You stay here, keep working. I will be two hours, no more.”

Anaïs studied him, then gave a single, reluctant nod. “Be the administrator of this, too. Be careful.”

*

The Brussels night was clear and cold. The administrative rain had been temporarily suspended. Léopold took a circuitous route, his senses heightened, watching for any sign of surveillance. He saw nothing out of the ordinary—just the normal, quiet bustle of the city on a weeknight.

His building was a solid, respectable edifice near the Square Ambiorix. He entered not through the grand front door, but through a small, iron gate that led to a delivery alley and a basement entrance. He used a key few knew existed. The basement was a labyrinth of storage rooms and washing facilities. He climbed the back stairs to his floor, emerging into the carpeted hallway. It was silent.

His apartment door was undisturbed. He unlocked it and stepped inside, immediately enveloped by the familiar scent of old paper, lemon wood polish, and solitude. For a moment, he simply stood, the contrast between this ordered silence and the charged, collaborative intensity of the notary’s office striking him with physical force. This was his life. Neat, contained, comprehensible.

He shook off the feeling. He went to his study, opened the safe concealed behind a false panel of bookshelves, and retrieved the manuscript of Blanchard’s journals. He packed a small bag with clothes and his own notebooks. He was efficient, methodical. He allowed himself one glance at his desk, at the neat piles of correspondence, the serene blank screen of his computer. He felt a pang, not of longing, but of farewell. He was leaving this version of himself behind.

He was at the door, bag in hand, when he heard the softest of sounds from the hallway. Not a footstep, but the faint creak of a floorboard under weight. He froze, his hand on the door handle. He did not breathe.

Through the peephole, the hallway was empty. But the quality of the silence had changed. It was a listening silence.

He backed away from the door, his heart a cold, hard stone in his chest. They were here. Or someone was. They had not forced entry; they were waiting. For him to leave, perhaps. Or for him to return. His mind raced. The service entrance was his only option. He moved silently through the apartment, back to the kitchen and the door to the back stairs.

As he reached for the handle, his phone, set to silent, vibrated in his pocket. He pulled it out. A message from an unknown number. The text was brief, and it turned the cold stone in his chest to ice.

*“The notary’s office in the Marolles has lovely old doors. But the locks, I’m told, are a century old. A matter of historical interest. How is the research progressing, Monsieur Vandenberg? We would so like to compare notes. – A friend of the family Dumont.”*

They knew. They had not just found his apartment. They had found the sanctuary. And they had not broken in. They were inviting him to open the door.

He looked at the message, then at the door to the back stairs. They would be waiting there too. It was a pincer movement, elegant and thorough. He was not just a fugitive; he was a piece on a board, and the other side had just revealed they knew the position of every piece.

He thought of Anaïs and Kofi, unaware, working by the green-shaded lamp. He thought of Luzolo’s drawing pinned to the wall. The stone meant to change the river’s course.

The next move was his. And he had to make it alone.



---

# Chapter 5

## **CHAPTER 5**

The Marolles district did not remember Belgium. It remembered a time before maps, a stubborn, sedimentary place where the cobbles seemed to rise like the vertebrae of a buried beast. The rain here was different. It was not administrative; it was accusatory, finding the gaps in the eaves of leaning houses, dripping from rusted signs in a slow, patient interrogation. Léopold stood under the awning of a shuttered bakery, the scent of old yeast and damp stone clinging to the air. His satchel was a lead weight against his side. The message on his phone was a burning coal in his pocket.

He had not taken the back stairs. He had taken the only route left: forward, into the open. He had walked for an hour, circuitous and watchful, a ghost moving against the current of the evening rush. He had sent a single, coded text to Anaïs—*“The archive is damp. Review postponed.”*—a warning to secure the workshop, to vanish. He received no reply. The silence was either discipline or disaster.

The notary’s office was across the narrow street, squashed between a pawnshop and a café that sold only beer and hard-boiled eggs. Its door was as described: a massive, blistered thing of oak and black iron, studded with nails. A sign, faded to illegibility, hung from a single chain. It was less a business than a geological feature. The windows on the upper floor were opaque with grime.

He was not alone. He had known he would not be. They were not following him; they were herding. A man in a fawn-coloured trench coat had been a still point on the platform of the Bourse metro station. A woman with a blue shopping bag had turned the same corner three times, her pace never altering. They were not hiding. They were displaying their presence, a calm and open surveillance that was more unnerving than any furtive pursuit. It was the gaze of a curator observing a specimen finally delivered to its case.

He crossed the street. The act felt less like a decision and more like a physical inevitability, a stone succumbing to gravity. His hand, when he raised it to the iron knocker, was steady. The knock was a dull, swallowed sound, as if the door were a foot thick.

It was opened not by a clerk, but by a man who looked like a retired stevedore, his shoulders straining the seams of a tweed jacket, his eyes the colour of old ice. He said nothing, merely stepped back, granting entry to a gloom that smelled of beeswax, mildew, and something metallic underneath.

“Monsieur Vandenberg,” a voice said from the shadows at the end of the hall. It was a cultivated voice, smooth as poured tar. “We were beginning to think you preferred the rain.”

The hallway gave onto a large, high-ceilinged room that had once been a counting house. A single green-shaded lamp burned on a vast, scarred desk, its light failing to reach the corners where darkness pooled like ink. Bookshelves, crammed with ledger-bound volumes, climbed the walls. In the centre of the room, standing beside the desk as if he were its natural companion, was a man of perhaps sixty. He was impeccably dressed in a charcoal suit, his silver hair swept back from a forehead that was pale and unlined. He held a letter opener, its blade catching the lamplight as he turned it idly in his hands. It was not a threat; it was a statement. He was a man accustomed to opening things.

“Please,” the man said, gesturing to a leather chair facing the desk. “Sit. You have had a trying afternoon.”

Léopold did not sit. He let his satchel hang from his shoulder. “A friend of the family Dumont, I presume.”

“A friend to the memory of the family,” the man corrected, placing the letter opener on the desk with a soft click. “A steward of their legacy. My name is Séverin. I am, among other things, an executor of complicated estates.”

“And I am a researcher of obscure history.”

Séverin’s smile was a thin, professional curve. “We are both men who sift through the debris of the past, then. The difference is one of ownership. You sift for knowledge. I sift for property.”

He moved to the side of the desk and leaned against it, the picture of casual authority. “The Dumont holdings in the Congo were vast. A kingdom of rubber and ivory and mineral rights. When the wind of change blew through Africa, it blew away many things. Records. Receipts. Certain… understandings. What remained was a labyrinth of legal fictions and missing covenants. My life’s work has been to reconstruct that labyrinth. To restore what was dispersed.”

Léopold felt a cold clarity settle over him. This was not a thief in the night. This was the bureaucracy of plunder, grown old and refined. “And the Luzolo Codex? Where does it fit in your restoration?”

“Ah.” Séverin’s eyes gleamed in the lamplight. “The Codex is not a property. It is a key. The Dumont concession maps were drawn by men who used rivers and hills as boundaries. But the true borders, the ones that mattered for the sub-surface rights, were described in relation to sacred sites—groves, waterfalls, stones that were meant to change a river’s course. The colonial surveyors, in their haste, transcribed these references as folklore, as local colour. But the Codex, your Father Luzolo’s life’s work, decodes them. It translates the spiritual geography into a cartography of material wealth. With it, one can read the land as the Kôngo elders read it. And thus, one can claim what lies beneath it with an unassailable, ancient legitimacy.”

The horror of it was its elegance. It was not just stealing land; it was weaponizing the soul of the land to justify the theft. A perfect, closed circle of exploitation.

“You want to use a testament of resistance as a deed of ownership,” Léopold said, his voice flat.

“I want to complete a transaction that was interrupted by history,” Séverin replied. “The Dumont family had a contract with the land. The Codex is the missing signature. You have done remarkable work, Monsieur Vandenberg. Your translation is nearly complete. We have followed your progress with great interest. The scholar you hired in Kinshasa, the quiet work in your little archive… it has saved us a great deal of time.”

“They are not part of this.”

“Everyone is part of this,” Séverin said, pushing himself off the desk. “It is the nature of an estate. It encompasses all who touch it. Now. You have a choice. You can give me the manuscript, your notes, and the location of your workshop. In return, you and your associates will be compensated. Handsomely. You can walk away, your research concluded, your curiosity satisfied.”

“And the other choice?”

Séverin picked up the letter opener again. “The other choice is that I take them. And the estate will have to be… liquidated. Including its recent acquisitions.”

The silence in the room was absolute, a listening silence, just like in his apartment hallway. It was the silence of the archive, of ledgers that recorded atrocities in neat, copperplate handwriting. Léopold looked at the man before him, a custodian of a dead family’s hunger, and he understood the true shape of the conflict. It was not a chase. It was a probate hearing. And he was a disputed asset.

“I need to speak to my team,” he said.

“Of course,” Séverin said, as if granting a reasonable request. “But you will do it here. And you will do it now.”

He nodded to the stevedore by the door, who produced Léopold’s own phone from his pocket and placed it on the desk. The screen was dark. Dead.

“A precaution,” Séverin said. “Use the landline. The number is pre-dialled. Speak plainly. They are waiting for your call.”

Léopold looked at the heavy, black telephone on the desk. It was an artifact. A trap. He picked up the receiver. It was warm, as if someone had just been listening on the other end. He heard the dial tone, a low, European hum.

He thought of Anaïs’s fierce concentration, of Kofi’s gentle hands on the fragile paper. He thought of the stone and the river. He was not a piece on their board. He was the stone. And a stone had only one purpose: to change the course.

He did not dial. He brought the heavy receiver down, with all his strength, onto the corner of the oak desk.

The crack was explosive in the silent room. The brittle black plastic shattered, the casing splitting to reveal a tangle of coloured wires and a small, sleek listening device that was not part of any landline’s normal anatomy. It skittered across the wood and fell to the floor.

For a fraction of a second, Séverin’s polished composure fissured. His eyes went to the broken device, then to Léopold’s face. It was not anger Léopold saw there. It was a profound, weary disappointment. The disappointment of a archivist finding a priceless document defaced.

“A costly gesture,” Séverin said softly.

The stevedore took a step forward. From the deep shadows at the back of the room, two other shapes emerged, silent and solid.

“The manuscript,” Séverin said, his voice still calm, but now devoid of its professional warmth. It was the voice of a judge passing sentence. “And the location. Now.”

Léopold backed away, his shoulder hitting a bookshelf. The ledger-books felt like bricks against his back. He had no weapon. No escape. He had only the satchel, and the truth inside it.

“It’s not here,” Léopold said.

“We will see.”

Séverin nodded. The stevedore moved with startling speed. His hands, large as spades, gripped the leather satchel and tore it from Léopold’s shoulder. The strap snapped with a sound like a gunshot. The man dumped the contents onto the desk.

Out spilled Léopold’s notebook, his pencils, a water bottle, a packet of biscuits. And a ream of paper, neatly bound with a rubber band.

Séverin picked it up, slipped off the band, and fanned the pages. His eyes scanned them, faster and faster. His expression, for the first time, showed genuine confusion, then dawning, cold fury.

Every page was blank.

“A decoy,” Léopold said, his breath short. “The research is safe. You can’t have it.”

Séverin let the blank pages flutter to the floor. He looked at Léopold not as a rival, but as a problem in logistics. “Then you will reproduce it. You will be provided with every comfort. Every incentive.”

“I won’t.”

“You will,” Séverin said. He picked up the letter opener. “Because if you do not, we will find your workshop. We will find your friends. And we will bring them here. And you will watch as I provide the incentives.”

He gestured to the men. “Take him to the quiet room. Let him consider the geometry of his situation.”

The stevedore’s hand clamped on Léopold’s arm. The grip was absolute, pinching nerve and bone. The other two moved in. Léopold struggled, a futile, animal motion. As they dragged him towards a small, iron-bound door set into the back wall, his eyes swept the room, desperate for an anchor. They caught on the ledger on the top of the nearest shelf. Its spine was labelled, not with a name, but with coordinates and a date: *1927. Secteur VII. Rendement.*

The quiet room was a vault. Windowless, airless, lined with old safe deposit boxes, their doors hanging open like empty mouths. A single bulb in a wire cage cast a jaundiced light. They shoved him inside. The heavy door swung shut with a final, resonant *thud* that was not the sound of wood, but of steel.

The lock engaged with a sound like a bone breaking.

Silence descended, thicker and heavier than any he had ever known. It was the silence of a tomb, of a ledger closed for the last time. He was in the innermost cave. The stronghold was not a place of violence, but of administration. His ordeal was not to face a monster, but to become a clerk in the service of one.

He sank to the cold stone floor, his back against the boxes. He thought of the blank pages spilling across the desk. It had been a gamble, a last-second switch in his apartment as he heard the creak in the hallway. The real manuscript, his notes, were hidden in the one place he hoped they would not think to look: in the basement of the philosophy café, taped beneath the table where he had first met Anaïs. A parenthesis of stillness.

He had protected the knowledge. But he had delivered himself. And in doing so, he had painted a target on everyone he knew. Séverin did not need to torture him. He only needed to wait. Eventually, Anaïs or Kofi would try to make contact. They would leave a digital trace, a physical pattern. They would become a thread, and Séverin’s men would pull it.

The stone had changed the river’s course, but it had been submerged in the process.

He did not know how long he sat there in the humming silence. Time became a flat, featureless plain. Then, a new sound. Not from the door, but from above. A faint, rhythmic scraping. Metal on stone. It was coming from one of the ventilation grilles high in the wall, a dark square barely visible in the gloom.

The scraping grew more insistent. Then, a whisper, frayed and urgent, filtered down through the metal ducts.

“*Léopold?*”

It was Anaïs.

His heart slammed against his ribs. “Anaïs? How—”

“*Kofi tracked your phone’s last ping. He’s a wizard with signals. We’ve been on the roofs for an hour. There’s a service hatch above you. Can you reach it?*”

He stood on trembling legs. The grille was eight feet up. The safe deposit boxes were his only ladder. He climbed, the metal frames groaning under his weight. He reached the grille, his fingers finding the slats. It was fixed with four rusted screws.

“*I have no tools,*” he whispered upwards.

A moment later, a small, precise object clattered down through the grille, bounced off his shoulder, and landed on the floor with a metallic ring. He climbed down, retrieved it. It was a Swiss Army knife, the red casing smudged with dirt. Kofi’s.

He climbed back, his hands shaking. It took an eternity of silent, straining work, his fingers cramping, but one by one, the screws turned, their threads shrieking in protest. The fourth one fell away into the darkness below.

He pulled the grille free. Beyond was a narrow vertical shaft, smelling of dust and pigeons. A rope, knotted at intervals, dangled into view.

“*Now,*” Anaïs’s voice hissed, strained with effort. “*Quickly.*”

He hauled himself up, the rope burning his palms. The shaft was a tight, dark throat. He climbed towards a sliver of grey night sky, towards the sound of the Marolles wind, which now sounded like freedom. He emerged onto a steep, slate roof, the city a panorama of glittering black and gold below. Anaïs was there, braced against a chimney stack, her face pale and set, holding the rope tied around her waist. Kofi was a few meters away, peering over the roof’s edge towards the street, a look of intense concentration on his face.

“They have a man at the front and back,” Kofi whispered without turning. “They are bored. They are not looking up.”

Anaïs helped Léopold onto the roof. Her grip was fierce. “The manuscript?”

“Safe. You shouldn’t have come.”

“You sent a ‘damp archive’ warning,” she said, her eyes scanning his face in the gloom. “That means the foundation is compromised. We are the foundation. We relocate.”

It was a statement of fact. There was no debate.

“They know about the workshop,” he said.

“Then the workshop is already gone,” Kofi said softly, finally turning. In his hand was not a phone, but a small, black device with a single switch. “I left a small gift for them. A data-scrubber hooked to a thermal battery. If they open the door, or if the temperature rises from too many bodies… it turns everything inside to digital ash.”

He saw the look on Léopold’s face. “The Codex is not the only text that must not be captured,” Kofi said. “Our research, our sources… it is a map to living people. It is a ledger, too. We could not let it be read.”

They had sacrificed their sanctuary. Their life’s work. To protect the secret, and to protect him.

“Where do we go?” Léopold asked, the wind plucking at his words.
