Anaïs looked out over the rooftops, towards the south-east. “Forward. The Codex points to a place. The heart of the old Dumont concession. If Séverin wants to use it as a key, we must get there first. We must become the lock.”

“It’s the most dangerous place we could go,” Léopold said.

“It is the only place left,” she replied.

They moved across the rooftops, a slow, precarious ballet over the abyss of the sleeping city. They were no longer researchers. They were fugitives in a war of inheritance. They had entered the stronghold and escaped, but they had left something vital behind in that quiet room: the illusion that the past was a country they could visit and then return from. They were in it now, up to their necks. The colonial aftermath was not a chapter in a book. It was the ground beneath their feet, and it was giving way.

As they lowered themselves down a rusted fire escape into a stinking alley, Léopold looked back at the dark bulk of the notary’s building. In a top-floor window, just for a second, he saw a figure silhouetted against the light. Séverin. Watching. Not with rage, but with the calm, resolute patience of a man who knows that estates are settled over decades, not days. He had lost a piece, but the board was still his.

The message was clear. The invitation was still open. And the family Dumont had a very long memory.



---

# Chapter 6

## **CHAPTER 6**

The road to the old Dumont concession was not a road at all, but a memory of one. The asphalt had long since surrendered to the equatorial forest, leaving a scar of crushed laterite the colour of dried blood. The jungle on either side was a solid, breathing wall of green. It did not watch them so much as absorb them, their progress a minor irritation in its vast, patient digestion of all things. The air in the Land Rover was a broth of heat and diesel fumes. Kofi drove, his eyes fixed on the ruts that tried to steer them into the ditch. Anaïs navigated with a pre-independence survey map, its creases worn soft as cloth. Léopold sat in the back, the Codex a leaden weight on his knees. He felt the text thrum through the leather, a silent, persistent frequency.

They had driven for two days, a tense, silent pilgrimage. The notary’s building, Séverin’s silhouette in the window—these had receded into a cold, administrative distance, like the details of a filed grievance. The immediate world was reduced to the jarring lurch of the vehicle, the whine of insects against the glass, and the oppressive weight of the green. This was not a place one visited. It was a place one was consumed by.

“Here,” Anaïs said, her voice a crack in the silence. She pointed to a break in the green that was less a path than a suggestion. “The old service track to the processing plant. It should bring us to the heart.”

Kofi turned the wheel, and the Land Rover groaned in protest, nosing into the green gloom. Vines scraped along the roof with a sound like whispered secrets. The light turned submarine, dappled and unreliable. After a kilometre, the track gave out entirely, swallowed by a giant *fromager* tree whose roots had erupted through the earth in great, knuckled waves. They had reached the end of driving.

They gathered their packs in the fungal half-light. The silence here was different. It was not an absence of sound, but a compression of it—the drip of water, the rustle of something unseen, the low hum of life—all pressed into a single, dense blanket. Léopold shouldered his pack, the Codex now against the small of his back. It felt less like a book and more like a graft, a new, unfamiliar organ.

“The map shows a clearing, five hundred metres due east,” Anaïs whispered, as if the forest might overhear and rearrange itself. “The manager’s villa was there.”

They began to walk. The ground was a mattress of decaying leaves, giving way underfoot with a soft, sighing collapse. The air smelled of rich rot and something else, something metallic and faintly sweet, like forgotten machinery. Léopold’s senses, honed in the dry archives of Brussels, were overwhelmed. This was the archive’s dark inverse. Here, history was not catalogued; it was compost.

They found the clearing not by sight, but by a sudden, shocking absence of canopy. The sky was a white, hot plate above a rectangle of struggling grass and low, thorny scrub. And in the centre, not a villa, but its ghost.

The manager’s house was a skeleton picked clean by time and lianas. Only the stone foundations and a single, defiant chimney stack remained, the brick stained with lichen like old maps. The rest was a tumble of rotten timber and corrugated iron, rusted to a lacework of holes. It was not a ruin that spoke of grandeur lost, but of something hastily abandoned and eagerly reclaimed. At its edge, half-swallowed by a flowering vine, stood a concrete plinth. On it, the bronze statue of a man in a colonial administrator’s uniform had been toppled. It lay face-down in the dirt, one arm outstretched, its hand forever pointing towards the deeper forest.

Anaïs walked to the plinth. She brushed away the crust of dead leaves from its inscription. Her finger traced the letters. “*À la mémoire d’Étienne Dumont, et à la civilisation qu’il apporta.*” She did not translate. She didn’t need to. The civilisation he brought was the vine now strangling his monument.

Kofi circled the clearing’s perimeter, his movements quiet, hunter-like. “This is not right,” he murmured.

“The map is clear,” Anaïs said.

“Not the place. The silence.” He stopped, listening. “No birds. Nothing.”

It was then that Léopold saw the wires. Almost invisible against the grey sky, a single strand of new, galvanised steel ran from the top of the remaining chimney stack into the wall of trees opposite. A modern filament stitched into the old wound.

“Anaïs,” he said.

She followed his gaze. Her face hardened. “A tripwire. For a camera.”

The clearing, then, was not abandoned. It was a exhibit. A baited trap.

From the forest, a man stepped into the light. He was tall, dressed in khaki trousers and a sweat-darkened shirt, but he moved with none of the heat-slowed languor of the climate. His movement was economical, precise. He carried no weapon in his hands, which only made him more threatening. He was followed by two others, emerging from different points in the tree line with the synchronicity of a rehearsed manoeuvre. They fanned out, blocking the paths back to the track.

The tall man’s face was narrow, impassive. He had the eyes of a bookkeeper assessing a ledger. “Monsieur Voss,” he said, his French accented with a South African flatness. “Mademoiselle Leroux. You have saved us a great deal of time. Mr. Séverin was certain your scholarship would lead you here.”

Léopold felt the weight of the Codex against his spine. It was no longer a key. It was a target painted on his back.

“Séverin’s men are accountants and lawyers,” Anaïs said, her voice steady, cold. “You are not.”

“No,” the man agreed. He gave a small, almost imperceptible nod. “We are the executors of the will.”

From behind the chimney stack, two more figures appeared, dragging a third between them. Kofi. His lip was split, and one eye was already swelling shut. He struggled, but the men held him with professional ease.

“Your guide is quite resilient,” the tall man said. “But this is not his theatre. This is a family matter.”

Léopold’s mind raced, a frantic, useless skittering. The philosophical certainties of the café, the careful textual analysis—all of it was ash here. This was a different grammar, one of force and possession.

“What does Séverin want?” Léopold asked, hearing the hollow academic tone in his own voice, so terribly out of place.

“The Codex, of course. The final piece of the testament. And you, Monsieur Voss. Alive. You are, as he put it, the only one who speaks the language. You will translate the final instructions on site.”

“And if I refuse?”

The man looked past him, to where Anaïs stood rigid by the plinth. “Then we begin removing your reasons to refuse. One by one.”

The threat hung in the humid air, more concrete than the statue on the ground. This was the colonial aftermath. It was not a debate in a journal; it was a man in khaki offering a binary choice in a clearing where the birds did not sing.

“Give me the book,” the tall man said, extending a hand.

Léopold’s fingers went to the straps of his pack. He saw Anaïs’s eyes, wide with a furious, helpless warning. He saw Kofi, sagging between his captors. He saw the toppled statue, pointing its bronze finger into the waiting dark. The illusion of choice was just that—an illusion. Séverin had not chased them. He had herded them. They had walked, of their own will, into the final clause of the contract.

He slid the pack from his shoulders. The leather was warm from his sweat. He held it out.

As the tall man took it, a smile touched his lips, a thin crack in his professional veneer. It was the smile of a job completed efficiently. He unzipped the pack, peered inside at the worn leather binding, and gave another nod.

“Good. Now, we proceed to the true site. The villa was always a decoy. The heart of the concession is deeper. Where the rubber was actually processed. Where the wealth was… extracted.”

He gestured with the pack towards the direction the statue pointed. One of his men produced a machete from a sheath on his back and began hacking a fresh path into the green wall. The sound was a violent, rhythmic tearing.

They were marched into the forest. Kofi was shoved ahead, a stumbling hostage. Anaïs walked beside Léopold, her arm brushing his. When she spoke, her voice was so low it was almost inaudible, just a vibration in the air between them. “The data-scrubber. In Brussels. It had a sibling device. A beacon. I kept it.”

He glanced at her. Her face was a mask of dirt and resolve.

“It is in my pocket. If I activate it, it will send a pulse. To anyone listening, it will scream our location. But Séverin will hear it too. It will tell him we are here, exactly where he wants us. It is a surrender.”

“Or a distraction,” Léopold breathed back.

She gave the faintest shake of her head. “There is no one to hear it but him.”

The path descended. The air grew cooler, damper, and the metallic smell intensified, now mixed with a chemical tang that caught in the throat. The forest began to change. The towering hardwoods gave way to smaller, twisted trees, their leaves coated in a fine grey dust. The ground underfoot became black and sticky in patches. They were walking into a poisoned place.

They emerged into a second, larger clearing. This one was not natural. It was a wound.

Before them lay the ruins of the processing plant. It was a sprawling, low complex of crumbling brick and corroded steel vats the size of small houses. Conveyor belts, now festooned with creepers, angled drunkenly towards rusted silos. A network of narrow-gauge railway tracks, red with oxide, vanished into tunnels in the forest. It was a cathedral of industry, deconsecrated and left to the jungle. The silence here was absolute, a vacuum. Nothing grew in the black earth between the structures.

In the centre of this desolation stood a single, intact building. It was a squat, windowless blockhouse of reinforced concrete, its roof studded with rusted ventilation pipes. It looked less like a building and more like a bunker, a fortress designed to protect something within from the world outside—or to protect the world from what was inside.

“The strong room,” the tall man said. “Where the weekly yield was tallied and stored before shipment. The family’s heart. Mr. Séverin believes the final instruction points here.”

He led them to a heavy, steel door, pockmarked with rust but still formidable. A modern keypad, sleek and anachronistic, was mounted beside it, a tiny green light winking in the gloom.

“Now, Monsieur Voss. The translation. The final coordinates, the combination. You will provide them.”

He handed Léopold the Codex. The book felt alien now, complicit. Léopold opened it to the last vellum page, the one he had pored over in the safe light of his Brussels flat. The symbols there were not directions to a place, but to a sequence. A series of numbers and letters derived from Dumont’s personal cipher, referencing ledger books, shipment dates, mortality quotas. It was a code within a code, a ledger of suffering translated into a lock combination.

His hands trembled. He was not translating a text. He was performing a ritual of inheritance. He was opening the door for Séverin.

He read the sequence aloud, his voice echoing flatly off the concrete. The tall man input the numbers. With a deep, hydraulic *clunk*, the door’s locking mechanism disengaged.

The man pushed the heavy door inward. It swung open without a sound, revealing a darkness so complete it seemed solid. A breath of air sighed out—cold, dry, and carrying the unmistakable scent of old paper, ink, and something else: the faint, sweet smell of preserved tobacco and dried flowers. The smell of a study, hermetically sealed for decades.

The tall man took a powerful torch from his belt and shone it inside. The beam cut through the darkness, illuminating a room frozen in time. A large, teak desk. A leather chair. Filing cabinets along one wall. And on the desk, a simple, polished wooden box, about the size of a large ledger.

“Wait here,” the tall man ordered his men. He glanced at Kofi and Anaïs. “Keep them.” Then, to Léopold: “You. With me.”

Léopold stepped across the threshold. The air was twenty degrees cooler. The door swung shut behind them with a final, resonant *boom*, sealing them in. The torch beam was the only source of light, a frantic white eye darting over the relics.

The man went straight to the box. He set the torch on the desk, its beam pointing upward, casting their faces in grotesque shadow. He did not open the box immediately. Instead, he looked at Léopold.

“Mr. Séverin wished for you to see this,” he said. “To understand the scale of the estate you are interfering with. To know what you are choosing to walk away from.”

He lifted the lid.

Inside, nestled on a bed of faded velvet, was not a document. It was a collection of stones. Rough, uncut diamonds the size of pigeon’s eggs, their facets catching the torchlight in cold, hard flashes. Beside them, neatly bundled stacks of bearer bonds from a dozen pre-war European banks. And a single, yellowed parchment, on which was drawn not a map, but a family tree. The Dumont lineage, with blank spaces where certain names might have been. A genealogy of power.

“This is the reserve,” the man said. “The emergency fund, in case the political winds shifted. Which they did. This…” he gestured around the room, “is the true archive. The real ledger. Not of rubber, but of influence. Of payments made, officials owned, silence purchased. This is what built the fortune that built Brussels. And it is what will secure its future.”

He looked at Léopold, and in that moment, Léopold saw not a mercenary, but a true believer. A priest of this dark sacrament. “You have a choice, Monsieur Voss. You can be the scholar who helped restore the Dumont legacy to its rightful steward. You can have a share of this. A life of quiet, profound significance. Or you can be the footnote. The obstruction that was removed.”

He picked up one of the diamonds, held it up to the light. It glittered with a cruel, beautiful fire. “The Codex was the key. But this… this is the lock. The thing that seals the world as it is, as it should be. Do you understand?”

Léopold understood perfectly. This was the culmination. Not of his research, but of the system his research had naively sought to critique. It was invulnerable. It had metabolised critique, violence, history itself, and turned it into capital. It offered him a place within its immutable ledger. All he had to do was accept the terms.

He felt a collapse within him, a structural failure. The moral certainty of the archive, the righteous anger of the activist—it was all just paper, thin and flammable. Against this concrete, diamond-hard reality, it was nothing. He was nothing. A man with a book, standing in a tomb of perfect, preserved power.

He looked at the diamond, at the bonds, at the family tree. He thought of Anaïs and Kofi outside, of the long, slow, patient reach of Séverin. He thought of the grey rain in Brussels, the administrative mist. It was all the same system. He was inside it now. He had always been inside it.

He nodded, a slow, heavy motion. “I understand.”

The man smiled, a genuine expression of satisfaction. He replaced the diamond. “Good. Then we are concluded. Mr. Séverin will be pleased.”

He took the wooden box, tucked it under his arm, and picked up the torch. He gestured for Léopold to precede him back to the door.

As Léopold turned, his foot brushed against something under the desk. Not a box or a book, but a small, cylindrical object. A tin. He stooped, as if to steady himself, and palmed it in the darkness, slipping it into his pocket. It was an instinct without hope, the last, meaningless twitch of a defeated thing.

The man opened the heavy door. The blinding white heat and the smell of decay rushed in. Anaïs and Kofi were where they had been left, flanked by the guards. Anaïs’s eyes searched Léopold’s face. What she saw there—the hollowed-out acceptance—made her own face go slack with a despair more profound than fear.

“It is done,” the tall man announced to his team. “We have the asset. We will escort them back to the extraction point. Mr. Séverin will deal with them there.”

He led the way back through the necropolis of the factory. Léopold walked like a sleepwalker. The Codex was back in his pack, but it was empty now. Its meaning had been transferred to the box under the man’s arm. He had translated the final passage, and in doing so, had signed their sentence. He had chosen, and he had chosen wrong. He had chosen the world as it was.

They reached the edge of the poisoned clearing, where the healthy forest began. The tall man paused, consulting a GPS unit on his wrist.

It was then that Anaïs moved.

It was not an attack. It was a release. Her hand dove into her pocket and emerged with a small, black plastic remote. She did not look at Léopold. She looked at the sky, through the gap in the canopy, as if offering a prayer to a deaf god. And she pressed the button.

A single, silent pulse of data shot upward from the device. A scream in the electromagnetic spectrum.

The tall man spun, his professional calm fracturing into pure, operational fury. “You idiot! What have you done?”

“I told you,” she said, her voice clear and dead. “I surrendered.”

In the distance, from the direction of the old villa clearing, came the sound of an engine. Not the grumble of their Land Rover, but the higher-pitched whine of a modern vehicle. Then another. Séverin. He had been waiting, listening. The beacon was a dinner bell.

The tall man’s face calculated odds in a flash. His priority was the box. The primary asset. The people were now a liability, a complication with converging forces.

“Change of plan,” he barked at his men. “We go to the alternate extraction. Now. Leave them.”

“The woman activated a beacon, sir,” one of the guards said. “They’ll lead them right to us if we leave them alive.”

The tall man’s gaze swept over Léopold, Anaïs, and Kofi. It was the look of a man discarding trash. He raised his hand, a signal.

It was the moment of symbolic death. Not with drama, but with efficiency. Léopold saw the guard behind Kofi raise a pistol, fitted with a long, black suppressor. He saw the man beside Anaïs do the same. He was not looking at Léopold. Léopold was already dead. He had died in the strong room.

But the tin in his pocket. His fingers closed around it. He didn’t think. He acted. He yanked it out, fumbling with the lid. It was not hope. It was reflex.

The lid came off. Inside, not tobacco, but a fine, grey powder. Ash. And nestled in the ash, a single, dried, perfectly preserved orchid. A *Disa uniflora*. A ghost flower from a land far to the south. A pressed, fragile memory of beauty in a tin of dust.

He flung the contents—ash and flower—into the face of the guard nearest Anaïs.

The man flinched, coughing, the grey powder clouding his vision. The shot meant for Anaïs’s head went wide, *phut*-ing into the dirt.

The disruption was a split-second. A hiccup in the execution.

Kofi used it. He was not a scholar, not a fugitive. He was a man who had grown up in the aftermath, where hesitation was a luxury. As his guard’s attention flicked to the commotion, Kofi drove his elbow backward into the man’s sternum. There was a grunt of expelled air. The pistol wavered. Kofi spun, a movement of pure, desperate economy, and grabbed the weapon, forcing the man’s wrist upward. A second muffled shot tore through the leaves above.

Chaosis a vacuum. It rushes in to fill order.

Léopold didn’t see the fight. He saw the tall man, box under arm, turning to run into the forest towards his escape route. He saw the primary asset getting away, carrying the lock that would seal the world forever.

He did not charge. He did not shout. He simply bent down, picked up a fist-sized piece of jagged, rusted machinery from the factory floor, and threw it.

He had not thrown a ball since he was a boy in the manicured parks of Uccle. The arc was clumsy, weak. It would not reach.

It did not need to. It struck the corroded leg of a giant, tripod-mounted conveyor structure ten feet ahead of the running man. The structure gave a metallic groan, a sound of profound fatigue. A critical bolt, already hairline-fractured by decades of rust, sheared clean.

With a shriek of tearing metal, the entire towering assembly—twenty feet of iron latticework and rusted gears—collapsed sideways, a dead giant falling.

The tall man tried to dart clear. He almost made it. The main weight of the structure missed him. But a single, swinging steel girder, like the scything arm of a clock, caught him across the back of the legs.

There was a sickening, dry crack, louder than any suppressed gunshot. He went down without a cry, the wooden box flying from his grasp, spilling its diamonds and bonds onto the black earth like worthless glitter. He lay still, his legs at an impossible angle.

Silence rushed back in, thicker than before.

The remaining guard, the one Léopold had blinded with ash, was on the ground, disarmed, Kofi’s knee on his chest. The other was unconscious. The engines were closer now, snarling through the forest track.

Anaïs stood, breathing hard, staring at Léopold as if she had never seen him before. He was still holding the empty tin.

Kofi rose, the captured pistol in his hand. He looked at the fallen structure, at the broken man, at the scattered diamonds winking in the filth. He looked at Léopold.

“The lock,” Léopold said, his voice a rasp. He walked to where the wooden box lay. He did not look at the diamonds. He picked up the single sheet of parchment, the family tree. He looked at the blank spaces. Then he took the tin from his pocket, the tin that had held ash and a ghost orchid. He folded the parchment carefully and placed it inside, with the last remnants of grey powder. He closed the lid.

He had not seized the diamonds. He had taken the ledger. The true map.

“We cannot go back the way we came,” Kofi said, listening to the approaching engines. “They will be on the track.”

Anaïs pointed away from the sounds, away from the villa, deeper into the forest, towards the uncharted green. “Then we go where the map does not go.”

They ran. Not as fugitives, but as survivors. They left the strong room open, the diamonds in the dirt, the broken executor of the will. They crashed into the living wall of the jungle, leaving the dead factory behind.

As the sound of the pursuit faded into the white noise of insects and their own ragged breath, Léopold felt the tin in his pocket. It was not a victory. It was a theft. They had not won the war. They had stolen a single, vital page from the ledger. They had become, not the lock, but a loose thread in the fabric Séverin was trying to re-weave. They were in the aftermath now, truly. They were the unforeseen variable, the error in the inheritance. And they were deep in the green, with no way back, only a way forward into the unmapped dark, carrying a ghost flower and a list of blank spaces that demanded to be filled.



---

# Chapter 7

## **CHAPTER 7**

The jungle, in the aftermath, was a patient creditor. It did not demand immediate payment for their passage, but it logged the debt with meticulous care. A liana scraped across Léopold’s cheek, leaving a thin receipt of blood. A root twisted under Kofi’s boot, a whispered reminder of obligation. They ran not with the frantic energy of flight, but with the grim, plodding economy of those who know the account is being tallied behind them. The white noise of insects was the sound of the jungle counting.

They ran until the light bled from green to grey, and the ground began to slope upwards, away from the river’s damp breath. Anaïs found it first: a shelf of rock under a canopy so thick the rain became a rumour, a distant administrative decision they were no longer subject to. They collapsed into the space, not as into a sanctuary, but as into a temporary ledger, a line drawn to sum up the day’s catastrophic profit and loss.

For a long time, there was only the sound of their breathing, settling into the rhythm of the forest’s own respiration. Léopold’s hand went to his pocket. The tin was there, a cold, flat weight against his thigh. He did not take it out. To look at it here, in this green gloom, felt like reading a will in a stranger’s house.

Kofi broke the silence. He had the captured pistol disassembled on a leaf before him, cleaning the grit of the strong room floor from its mechanism with a methodical, surgical focus. “They will not follow tonight,” he said, his voice low. “The track is for vehicles. On foot, in this, they are clerks. We are the irregular entry.”

“They will have radios,” Anaïs said. She was binding a tear in her trousers with a strip of cloth, her movements efficient, unemotional. “The perimeter will be informed. The checkpoints on the roads to Kisangani, to Ubundu, will be waiting for three people. One white, two Black. Carrying…” She glanced at Léopold’s pocket. “Carrying something.”

“We are not carrying diamonds,” Léopold said. His own voice sounded foreign to him, rasped raw by adrenaline and dust.

“We are carrying a question,” Kofi corrected, slotting the pistol’s slide back into place with a soft, definitive click. “And to men like Séverin, a question is more dangerous than a gem. A gem has a fixed value. A question can unravel everything.”

They were the loose thread. The concept was no longer abstract. It was the ache in their muscles, the dirt under their nails, the hollow, gnawing fear that had settled where their hunger should be. Léopold finally drew out the tin. It was unremarkable, a cheap, lacquered thing, the red faded to a dull brick. He opened it. Inside, the folded parchment lay atop a residue of grey ash. The ghost orchid powder had mingled with the remains of his father’s ledger, creating a fine, silent silt. He lifted the parchment out, careful not to let it touch the ash.

In the failing light, the family tree was a stark, bureaucratic ghost. The names he knew—Séverin, his own father—were anchors in a sea of blank lines. Some blanks were for the dead, erased from the official record. Others, he now understood, were for the living who had been deemed assets, not ancestors. The lines connecting them were not of blood, but of transaction, of ownership disguised as kinship. It was a map of a different kind of inheritance, one written in silences.

“Here,” he said, his finger finding a blank space connected to a subsidiary line of the Séverin branch. A date, 1958, but no name. “And here.” Another, 1961. “These are not omissions. They are vacancies. Positions waiting to be filled.”

Anaïs leaned closer, her shoulder almost touching his. She smelled of sweat and crushed fern. “By people like the man in the strong room. The executor.”

“A functionary,” Léopold murmured. “A piece of the lock. Replaceable. The diamonds were the salary. This,” he tapped the parchment, “is the employment contract.”

Kofi had finished with the gun. He watched them, his eyes reflecting the deep green of the canopy. “So we have not stolen treasure. We have stolen a personnel file.”

“We have stolen the true map,” Léopold repeated his own words from the strong room, but they tasted different now, less like triumph, more like a diagnosis. The map did not lead to a place. It led to a system. A colonial aftermath not of flags and borders, but of hidden obligations, of debts transferred from the colonial ledger to the private one, with the interest compounded in silence.

“Then the reward we sought,” Anaïs said, her voice flat, “is knowledge that we are inside a machine. And the machine has noticed a piece is missing.”

It was not a celebration. It was an autopsy. But in the cool, damp air of the rock shelf, with the jungle holding its breath around them, it felt, for a moment, like a respite. They had claimed the thing. They understood its shape. It was a cold, comfortless understanding, but it was theirs. They shared the last of the water from Kofi’s canteen, the warm, metallic taste of it a sacrament of their shared damnation. Léopold re-folded the parchment into the tin. The lid closed with a sound like the period at the end of a long, damning sentence.

The respite lasted until dawn.

The jungle’s morning audit began with birds. Not songs, but sharp, querying calls that echoed through the canopy like clerks comparing notes. Then, a new sound threaded beneath them: the distant, persistent buzz of a small-engine aircraft.

They were on their feet before the first pass, pressed against the rock. The plane was a tiny, insectile shape against the brightening strip of sky visible through the leaves—a Cessna, painted in civilian colours. It did not circle widely. It traced a precise, grid-like pattern over the river valley behind them, then began to expand the search north, towards their slope.

“Spotter,” Kofi said, the word a bullet in the quiet.

The consequence of their victory was not a furious, blind pursuit. It was a methodological, enraged correction. Séverin was not sending thugs into the green. He was deploying a system. The strong room had been a private, hidden failure. Now, the apparatus was engaging: air, radio, the men at the checkpoints Anaïs had named. A clock had started ticking, its mechanism oiled by money and cold fury.

The Cessna made another pass, lower this time. Sun glinted off its windshield, a winking, all-seeing eye. They were a loose thread, and the machine had begun the process of re-weaving, of snipping them out.

“We cannot stay here,” Anaïs stated. It was not fear in her voice. It was the acceptance of a revised itinerary. “The grid will tighten. They will put men on the ground where the plane sees something. A broken branch, the ash of a fire.”

“The roads are a trap,” Kofi said. “The rivers are watched. The forest itself is now a ledger, and they are looking for the irregular entry.”

Léopold looked from the tin in his hand to the unmapped green ahead, a wall of tangled, indifferent life. The heart of the conflict was not the villa, nor the strong room. The heart was the system the parchment described, and they were now swimming in its bloodstream. To flee it, they had to move not away from it, but through its very substance, to a place it could not see.

“Then we go where the map does not go,” he said, echoing Anaïs’s words from the day before, but now they were a strategy, not a desperation.

“The map goes everywhere,” Kofi countered softly. “But it is thin in some places. Stretched. We must find a place the ledger forgot to index.”

He shouldered his pack, his gaze sweeping the terrain. He was not a philosopher of the café. He was a reader of a different text. “There are old trails. Not for logging, not for mining. Older. Used by people who left no strong rooms. They are not on his maps. They are stories. We need to find a story.”

The decision was made without a vote. It was the only equation that balanced. They would flee the heart of the conflict by disappearing into the one thing the colonial aftermath could never fully catalogue, the one asset it could never properly depreciate: the living, forgetting land itself.

They abandoned the rock shelf as the Cessna droned towards the east. Kofi took the lead, his navigation shifting from the avoidance of pursuit to the seeking of absence. He looked for signs not of path, but of memory—a subtle dip in the undergrowth, a tree with bark stripped in a pattern that spoke of a handrail from another century, a cluster of edible mushrooms that hinted at old, dispersed cultivation. He was reading the forest’s marginalia.

They moved in a silence that felt like theft. Each step away from the search grid was a pilfered moment. The ordinary world—the world of cafés and trams and administrative rain—was not a place to return to. It was the very fabric of the machine they were inside. Their chase was not back to it, but out of one of its deepest, most forgotten cells.

The day dissolved into a green kaleidoscope of fear and focus. The sound of the plane faded, replaced by the hammer of their own hearts. They drank from slick leaves when they found no stream, ate bitter berries Kofi identified with a curt nod. They were stripping themselves down to the essentials, becoming entries in a ledger of survival: calories consumed, distance covered, silence maintained.

As the second dusk began to stain the air indigo, they found the story.

It was a cleft in the earth, hidden by a curtain of cascading vines. Not a cave, but a split in a great laterite shelf, forming a shallow, sheltered overhang. And on the wall, faded to the colour of old blood, were paintings. Stick-figure men with spears, herds of antelope with elegant, looping horns, a sun with radiating lines. They were simple, ancient. They told of a hunt, of a life that had no boxes of diamonds, no family trees of control.

They stood before them, three ragged, modern ghosts. The paintings were a ledger too, but one of a different economy. Of meat and movement, of sun and rain. A system that had ended, not with a strong room, but with a forgetting.

“Here,” Kofi said, his voice hushed with something like reverence. “This place is not on his map.”

It was not a safe house. It was a parenthesis. They built no fire. They ate the last of their tough ration in the dark, under the gaze of the hunting party on the wall. The tin was between them, a cold, modern artifact on the ancient floor.

Léopold lay back, the laterite cool against his spine. The chase had begun. They were in the unmapped dark, carrying their blank spaces. He thought of the café in Brussels, the fogged windows, the smell of decayed paper. That had been the anteroom of this. The administrative rain there was the same substance as the system here, just a different climate.

He closed his eyes. The hook was set deep now, not in the mystery of his father’s death, but in the living, breathing consequence of his father’s life. They had stolen a page. The book was now chasing them. And somewhere ahead, in the green, forgotten dark, was the next blank space on the parchment, waiting to be filled not with a name, but with a choice. The machine would send its pieces to find them. And they, the loose threads, would have to decide whether to be snipped out, or to unravel everything they touched.



---

# Chapter 8

## **CHAPTER 8**

The green swallowed them whole. It was not the passive verdancy of a European forest, but a thick, breathing entity that pressed against the skin. The air in the cleft had been dry, a museum of dust and ochre pigment. Stepping back into the jungle at first light was like walking into a warm, wet mouth. Léopold felt it immediately: the administrative efficiency of the pursuit. It was in the sudden, synchronized silence of the birds that marked their passage like a dotted line on a map. It was in the snapped frond, too clean, left as a memorandum on the path behind them. The system here did not send memos; it sent men. But the principle was identical.

Ama led, her movements a fluid contradiction to the dense undergrowth. She did not fight it; she let it part for her, a secretary moving through familiar filing cabinets. Kofi was rearguard, his presence communicated only by the occasional soft exhalation or the creak of his leather satchel, now stuffed with empty tins and the weight of the stolen page. Léopold moved between them, a clumsy comma in the sentence of their flight. His own satchel, with its manuscript corner, thumped against his hip—a dead weight of questions in a world that now dealt only in consequences.

They followed no path, only Ama’s reading of the land’s subtle grammar: the tilt of a fern, the scuff on a liana, the distant, choked cough of a river. By mid-morning, the pursuit had crystallized from a notion into a sound. It was the metallic *snick* of a safety catch, disengaged somewhere in the green murk to their left. A professional’s error, or a professional’s message.

“They are herding us,” Ama whispered, not breaking stride. Her voice was low, a vibration in the humid air. “Away from the river. Towards the old plantation road.”

“A bottleneck,” Kofi grunted from behind.

Léopold’s mind, trained on ledgers, produced the image: a narrowing column of figures, a deliberate funnel towards a final, damning total. The jungle was the spreadsheet, and they were the errant data points being corralled for deletion.

They broke into a run. It was not a sprint, but a desperate, loping gait, dodging buttress roots that rose like the dark fins of submerged ledgers. The sounds behind them shed all pretense of stealth. There were shouts now, guttural and functional, and the thrash of bodies moving with blunt force through the green tape. A bullet tore a chunk of bark from a mahogany tree an arm’s length from Léopold’s head. The report was a flat, administrative slap against the damp air. It was not a warning shot. It was a line-item veto.

**Scene 1**

The old plantation road was a scar of packed laterite, bleeding red dust into the jungle’s edge. It was a relic of a simpler extraction, where the commodity had been rubber, not compliance. To cross it was to step into a ledger column titled ‘Exposed.’ They crouched in the ferns, the red dust settling on their damp clothes like a fine, rust-coloured pollen.

“We do not cross,” Ama stated, her eyes scanning the opposite tree line. “It is what they want.”

“We cannot go back,” Kofi said. “They have closed the door behind us.”

Léopold looked down the straight, ruthless line of the road. It spoke of order, of carts and quotas and overseers. It was the spine of the old system, and the new one had laid its trap along it. He saw movement, a shift of shadow among shadows. Then another. Pieces on a board.

“How many?” he asked, his voice sounding strangely calm, as if asking for a tally.

“Enough,” Ama said. Then she pointed with her chin, not her hand. “And there. The *gros légume*.”

A man stepped from the green onto the road a hundred yards to the south. He wore khaki that was too crisp, boots that were too clean. He carried no rifle, only a pistol holstered at his hip. He took a pair of binoculars from his chest and raised them slowly, a bureaucrat consulting a document. Even at this distance, Léopold recognized the posture: the entitled stillness, the certainty of survey. This was no local hireling. This was a manager. A problem-solver from the central office.

The man lowered his binoculars and spoke to the air. A moment later, a loudhailer crackled to life, its amplified voice stripping the dignity from the jungle’s silence.

“Monsieur Vandenberg. You have taken property that is not yours. This is a final audit. Step onto the road. Bring the woman and the archivist. The matter will be reconciled.”

The word *reconciled* hung in the air, a euphemism with a gunpowder scent. Léopold felt a cold clarity. His father’s world was not one of assassins in shadows. It was one of accountants with pistols, of balanced books achieved through erasure. The violence was just the final entry in the ledger.

“They will not wait long,” Kofi murmured. “They will flush us.”

As if on cue, the thrashing in the jungle behind them intensified. The herding was over. The cull was beginning.

Ama turned to Kofi, a silent conversation passing between them in the language of glances and slight tilts of the head. It was the exchange of people who had shared a thousand small obediences and a few large silences. Kofi gave a minute nod. He unslung his satchel and pressed it into Léopold’s hands.

“Take this,” he said. His voice held no tremor, only the solidity of a decision entered into a log.

“Kofi—” Ama began, but he cut her off with a look.

“I am a footnote to them,” he said, a faint, grim smile touching his lips. “A local citation. You are the contested text. And he,” he nodded at Léopold, “is the missing index. Footnotes are expendable. The narrative is not.”

Léopold understood with a sinking certainty. “No. We split up. We scatter their focus.”

“They have the focus,” Kofi said, his eyes already scanning the road, calculating angles. “I will provide a distraction. A loud, incorrect entry in their ledger.”

**Scene 2**

Before Léopold could form another protest, Kofi was moving. Not into the jungle, but parallel to it, staying just within the fringe of ferns. He moved with a sudden, shocking speed, a man shedding the weight of a lifetime of careful filing. He broke from the cover not in a stealthy crouch, but standing tall, and he did not run across the road. He walked.

He walked directly towards the crisp man in khaki, his hands held slightly away from his body, empty. It was the walk of a man approaching a supervisor’s desk. The amplified voice barked something unintelligible. Kofi kept walking. He began to speak, his voice not shouting, but projecting, clear and firm across the red dust.

“You! Manager! Your records are incomplete! The shipment of 1959—the so-called ‘cultural artifacts’—the manifest omits the human cost! I have the testimonies! I have the names!”

He was inventing, weaving a tapestry of plausible, damning detail. He was creating a new, distracting file. The khaki man stared, first in confusion, then in cold irritation. He gestured sharply. Two armed shapes detached from the tree line and began advancing down the road towards Kofi, their rifles raised.

Kofi stopped. He turned, as if to address the whole jungle, the hidden clerks of this violent bureaucracy. “They think history is a storage room! They think it can be locked! But it is a vine! It grows through the cracks!”

He looked back, for one final moment, towards the ferns where Ama and Léopold hid. His gaze found Ama’s. It held no farewell, only a simple, devastating transfer of responsibility. Then he reached into his jacket.

The khaki man shouted. The two guards raised their rifles.

Kofi’s hand came out. It did not hold a weapon. It held a small, cheap, petrol lighter. And a sheaf of papers.

The first shot rang out, a flat, final period. It missed. Kofi flicked the lighter. A small, blue flame appeared, a tiny, defiant clerk in the face of the archive. He touched it to the papers.

They caught with a soft *whump*. He did not throw them. He held the burning sheaf aloft, a torch of false, brilliant testimony. The second shot did not miss. It punched into his shoulder, spinning him around. But he kept his feet, the burning papers held high, a beacon of incorrect and glorious data.

“Run!” The scream was Ama’s, raw and guttural, torn from a place deeper than strategy.

She grabbed Léopold’s arm, her fingers like iron bands, and yanked him back into the swallowing green. He stumbled, the image of Kofi, a dark silhouette against a fist of flame, seared onto his vision. They crashed through the undergrowth, no longer caring for silence, driven only by the fuel of his sacrifice. Behind them, more shouts, a volley of shots—not at them, but at the burning, staggering figure on the road, drawing all eyes, all bullets, towards his culminating, erroneous entry.

**Scene 3**

They ran until the air in their lungs was fire and the world was a green blur. Ama did not weep. She set her grief into her muscles, using it to power through walls of creeper and over rotten logs. Léopold followed, the two satchels—his own and Kofi’s—banging against him, a double weight of ghosts. The sounds of pursuit had vanished, redirected, consumed by Kofi’s diversion.

But the land itself now conspired against them. They hit a wall of bamboo, thick as a prison gate. To go around would take hours they did not have. The river was a roar to their right, but it was a torrent, swollen by a rain they had not felt, its banks sheer and slick. They were trapped in a green cul-de-sac. The ledger was closing again.

Ama leaned against a tree, her chest heaving. She looked at the bamboo, then at the river, her face a mask of furious calculation. The manager’s voice seemed to echo in the humid air: *The matter will be reconciled.*

Léopold slid down against a tree trunk, the satchels clunking to the ground. He stared at them. One held the dead father’s mystery. The other held the dead friend’s empty tins and the stolen, vital page. A page of names. A page of power. A page that was, in this moment, useless.

*You are the contested text.*

Kofi’s words. He was not a man of action. He was a man of documents. His father had been a man of systems. The answer was not in his muscles, but in the grammar of control. The antagonist was not a monster, but a clerk. And what did a clerk fear?

Not a bullet. An audit.

He fumbled open Kofi’s satchel. His hands found the oilcloth pouch. He pulled out the single, thick parchment page. The list of names, the family tree of complicity. It was a weapon, but not as he’d imagined. Not a sword. A key. Or perhaps, a lock.

“What are you doing?” Ama hissed, her eyes on the bamboo thicket, expecting men to erupt from it.

“Changing the entry,” Léopold breathed. He pulled his father’s manuscript from his own satchel. The corner was dented, the pages damp at the edges. He ripped a blank sheet from the back. With a stub of pencil from Kofi’s bag, his hands shaking not with fear but with a terrible clarity, he began to write. He did not write a name. He wrote a title.

**CONFIDENTIAL – EYES ONLY.**
**Operational Directive: Omega Sanction.**
**Regarding: Breach at Sector 7 (See attached list of compromised assets, Page 17-B).**
**Directive: All field operatives in attached jurisdiction are hereby ordered to stand down. Await verification team from central command. Any pre-emptive action by local assets constitutes a violation of protocol and will be met with terminal sanction.**
**Authentication Code: [He left a blank line]**
**By order of the Audit Division.**

He signed it with a flourish, an illegible scrawl of pure authority. He folded it crisply, then folded the precious, stolen page of names inside it. He held the packet out to Ama.

“They are clerks,” he said, his voice thin but steady. “They follow procedure. They respect the chain of command. This,” he tapped the packet, “is a higher command. A recall notice. They will have to stop to verify it. To make a call. It will create a hesitation.”

Ama stared at the packet as if it were a live serpent. “They will see it is a forgery. The code is blank.”

“They will see the list inside first. The real list. That will give it weight. The blank code will read as a security precaution. It will make them nervous. Clerks are bred on nervousness.” He looked at the bamboo. “We need a delivery system.”

Ama’s eyes hardened. She took the packet. She moved to the river’s edge, scanning the furious current. She found what she needed: a thick, curved fragment of bark, like a shallow bowl. She placed the folded packet inside it. From a pouch at her belt, she took a small tin of pungent salve and smeared a daub on the edge of the bark. She struck a match from Kofi’s lighter, which she had instinctively pocketed. The salve caught, a small, steady, waterproof flame. A message in a burning boat.

She launched it into the main current. The little bark vessel, bearing its false directive and its true, incendiary cargo, caught the torrent and sped away, the flame a bobbing, official-looking beacon.

“It will reach the road bridge downstream in minutes,” she said. “Before they have finished with… before they regroup.”

They did not wait to see if it worked. They turned to the bamboo. With a last, shared look that held Kofi’s ghost between them, they began to fight their way through, not around. The stalks were like iron bars, but they bent, they splintered. They were no longer fleeing. They were executing a new protocol, one of their own devising. The sound that reached them, ten minutes later, as they emerged scratched and bleeding on the other side, was not of gunfire. It was the distant, angry squawk of a radio. The sound of bureaucracy, confused.

**Scene 4**

They crossed the border at a forgotten footpath, marked only by a termite-eaten post leaning like a tired sentinel. There was no fence, no checkpoint. The difference was in the air. It was not lighter, but it was differently heavy. The jungle here had been logged and replanted; the trees stood in soldier-straight rows, a plantation of future desks. The ordinary world.

They hit a dirt track, then a paved road. A battered lorry, carrying a load of pineapples, ground to a halt at Ama’s wave. The driver, a man with eyes that had seen too many official papers, asked no questions. They climbed into the back, among the sweet, prickly cargo, and let the machine carry them away.

The city, when it emerged, was not Brussels. It was a regional capital, a place of low, sun-bleached concrete and the constant thrum of generators. It was the ordinary world, but Léopold saw it now through a lens of aftermath. The administrative rain here was actual rain, a warm, torrential downpour that flooded the streets as they jumped from the lorry. He saw the water carrying red laterite dust into the gutters—the dust of the road, the dust of the cleft, the dust on Kofi’s burning papers. He saw the people moving under awnings, their lives a complex negotiation with invisible forms and unspoken rules. He saw the system not as a distant abstraction, but as a climate. It was in the resigned slump of a man waiting in a government queue, in the wary calculation of a market trader, in the very grid of the streets, laid down by a long-dead planner in a faraway office.

They found a room in a boarding house that smelled of disinfectant and old curry. It had a ceiling fan that stirred the heat without cooling it. Léopold laid the two satchels on the single, narrow bed. They were scuffed, stained with mud and something darker. He opened his father’s manuscript. The words seemed alien now, the philosophical musings of a man playing chess with ghosts while real pieces were swept from the board. He had written of structures, of control. He had never written of the smell of burning paper and blood on a red road.

Ama stood at the window, watching the rain cleanse nothing. Her stillness was different now. It was not the stillness of waiting, but of containment, holding a storm within a defined vessel.

“He knew,” she said, not turning. “He knew it was the only currency they would accept. A fact for a fact. A life for time.”

“We have the time,” Léopold said, the words ash in his mouth. “What do we do with it?”

She finally looked at him. Her eyes were not soft with grief, but sharp with its residue. “We have the page. A copy of it, in your head. We have your father’s book, which is a map of the machine’s mind. And we have a receipt,” she said, nodding at the satchels, at the empty space where Kofi should have been. “A very expensive receipt. We do not hide. We audit.”

The word hung in the room, turning the hum of the fan into the whirr of a microfilm reader. Léopold saw it then, not as a flight, but as a pivot. They had been a loose thread, threatening to unravel a small corner. Now, they had to become the shears. But to cut one thread in a vast tapestry was meaningless. You had to find the master thread, the one that, when pulled, would make the whole image blur.

He thought of the café in Brussels, the fogged windows, the smell of decayed paper. That had been the anteroom. This damp, anonymous room, with its smell of curry and loss, was the office. The real work began here. Not with uncovering a mystery, but with compiling a dossier. A counter-ledger. His father had written the theory. They would have to write the evidence.

The hook was no longer in the mystery. It was in the mechanism. They had forced a hesitation in the machine. A machine that hesitates is a machine that has a flaw in its programming. And somewhere, in a vault, in a strong room, in a sun-bleached ministry, was the source code. The original charter. The first, damning entry.

The rain outside eased to a drip. The ordinary world went about its business, buying pineapples, filling forms, breathing the climate. Léopold Vandenberg, son of a philosopher, friend of a dead archivist, ally of a woman who was a living indictment, opened a blank page. He picked up his pencil. He did not write a story. He wrote a heading.

**Preliminary Findings: Re: Systemic Irregularities, Post-Colonial Audit Division.**

The work was administrative. It was grey. It was the only thing left that mattered.



---

# Chapter 9

## **CHAPTER 9**

The summons arrived not by courier, but by omission. It was a silence where a sound should have been. For three days, they worked in the room above the spice merchant’s, the air thickening with the scent of cardamom and ledger-dust. Léopold’s preliminary findings grew into a taxonomy of whispers: redirected funds, vanished manifests, land grants that bloomed like inkblots on a map, consuming villages. Elara cross-referenced with a cold, surgical precision, her father’s annotated copy of *The Administrative Leviathan* open beside her like a surgeon’s manual. They were not hunting a ghost. They were performing an autopsy on a system that was very much alive, and which believed itself to be the very definition of health.

The silence was this: no one came. No police asked after a missing archivist. No ministry clerks inquired about a philosopher’s son gone to ground. The machine, having hesitated, did not stumble. It simply noted the anomaly and recalibrated its protocols. Their hiding place was not a secret; it was a quarantine. Léopold felt it on the fourth morning, looking out at the street where the rain had returned, a lighter, tropical dilution of the Brussels grey. The pineapple-seller did not look up at his window. The postman’s gaze slid over the door. They had been made peripheral. It was a subtler violence.

“They are waiting for us to declare ourselves,” Elara said, not looking up from a column of shipping codes. “To make a move that is recognizably within the system. A complaint, a withdrawal, a flight. Anything with a form number. Then they can file us.”

“So we do nothing?”

“We do the one thing the system cannot process.” She closed the ledger. “We submit to its authority.”

* * *

The Ministry of Cultural Harmonization was a whitewashed block on the edge of the old European quarter, a building that aspired to be a filing cabinet. Its columns were purely decorative, holding up nothing but an impression of permanence. The rain here was different. It was hot, and evaporated from the pavement with a hiss, leaving the air feeling laundered and thin. Léopold wore his only suit, which had grown musty in the damp room and now hung on him with the awkwardness of a borrowed costume. Elara wore a severe, navy dress, her hair pinned back so tightly it seemed to stretch the skin of her temples. They were playing their parts: the dutiful son, the professional widow. They carried their evidence not in satchels, but in two slim, official-looking portfolios.

The lobby was a cathedral of bureaucracy. A vaulted ceiling echoed with the click of heels on terrazzo. High on the walls, murals depicted the benevolent transfer of knowledge: scrolls passing from robed Europeans to attentive locals under a triumphant sun. The air smelled of floor wax and faintly of mildew, the sweet decay of old paper now institutionalized, sanitized. A young man with immaculate cuffs sat behind a vast, empty desk, his purpose purely ontological: to prove the desk was manned.

“We have an appointment,” Elara said, her voice not cutting the silence but filling a pre-existing slot within it. “With the Director of Archival Reconciliation.”

The young man did not look at them. His fingers danced over an invisible keyboard. “Name?”

“Vandenberg. And Ms. Elara Mbeki.”

The fingers paused. “The purpose of the meeting?”

“To regularize the archival status of the late Professor Kofi Mbeki,” Léopold said. The words tasted of dust. “And to discuss the posthumous transfer of certain research materials.”

The man nodded, as if this was the most ordinary of requests. He lifted a telephone receiver, murmured into it, and replaced it with a soft, definitive click. “Fourth floor. Room 417. You may proceed.”

The elevator was a brass cage that shuddered as it ascended, offering framed glimpses of each floor through its grille: rows of typists, a library of bound reports, a tea-room where men stood in quiet clusters. It was the café in Brussels, distilled, amplified, and rendered entirely legible. No philosophers here. Only functionaries. The obstinacy had been refined into procedure.

Room 417 was not an office, but a waiting room. A window looked out onto a blank courtyard wall. Two chairs, upholstered in a scratchy burgundy fabric, faced a large, empty desk. On the wall hung a portrait of the King, and a framed copy of the Ministry’s founding charter. They sat. The clock on the wall ticked with a sound like a steady drip.

Fifteen minutes passed. Then thirty. It was a test, Léopold realized. Not of patience, but of ontology. Did they have the substance to persist in this non-space? To believe in the reality of their own appointment? He watched Elara. She did not fidget. She studied the founding charter on the wall as if it were a poem, her eyes tracing the florid script.

After forty-seven minutes, a door they had not noticed—a seamless panel in the wood—swung open. A woman entered. She was in her fifties, dressed in a suit the colour of a dove’s wing. Her hair was a helmet of grey-streaked black. She carried no papers. She moved to the desk and sat, placing her hands flat on the blotter.

“Mr. Vandenberg. Ms. Mbeki. I am Director Verhoeven.” Her voice was cool, accentless, a voice from a newsreel. “My condolences on your losses. A tragedy. The archives are poorer for it.”

It was the word “losses,” plural, that was the first trap. Acknowledging a connection the official record did not, and perhaps never would, make. Léopold inclined his head. “Thank you. We’ve come to ensure their work is not… mislaid.”

“A noble aim.” Verhoeven’s eyes were pale, the colour of winter sky. They rested on the portfolios in their laps. “The Ministry’s purpose is reconciliation. The harmonization of historical narratives with contemporary administrative reality. It is delicate work. Often, well-meaning individuals bring us materials—fragments, really—that, without proper context, can create more discord than they resolve.” She leaned forward, a minute gesture. “Your father’s book, Mr. Vandenberg. A theoretical work. A *philosophical* apparatus. And the late Professor Mbeki’s annotations. Passionate, no doubt. But passion is not documentation. It is a point of view.”

Elara spoke, her tone matching the Director’s in temperature. “We are not here to submit points of view. We are here to submit a discrepancy.”

“A discrepancy.” Verhoeven repeated the word as if it were a mildly interesting specimen.

“Between the archived record of the Benga Valley agricultural initiative,” Elara said, “and the land registry maps, shipping manifests, and subsequent corporate tax exemptions held in related, but not cross-referenced, ministries. The paper trail suggests a philanthropic project. The collateral trail suggests a transfer of assets with a net value exceeding twenty million francs, redirected through a series of shell corporations ultimately registered to a holding company whose board is a subset of the original Harmonization oversight committee.”

She said it all without inflection, a recitation of facts. It was the language of the machine itself. Léopold watched Verhoeven’s face. Not a flicker. Only a slight, professional sadness.

“Ms. Mbeki, you are grieving. What you perceive as a ‘collateral trail’ is often the necessary scaffolding of development. Funds are routed, yes. Structures are created. This is the machinery of progress. It is not inherently nefarious. Your father, and Mr. Vandenberg’s father, they dealt in ideals. We,” she gestured to the room, the building, the silent clock, “deal in materials. In concrete and capital. The translation from one to the other is inevitably… messy.”

It was the second trap. The appeal to the adult world of complicated realities. The dismissal of the philosophical as childish, the administrative as soberly mature.

Léopold opened his portfolio. He did not pull out a sheaf of papers. He laid a single sheet on the desk. It was a clean copy of the page Kofi had died for—the charter of the *Société Anonyme*, the signatures, the royal seal. Not the original. A facsimile. But perfect.

“This,” Léopold said, “is not a point of view. It is a founding document. It is the source code, Director. It states, in its first clause, that the enterprise is constituted for ‘the perpetual and exclusive management of designated territories and their human resources, under royal charter, answerable only to its own board.’ It is a state within a state. A license. And every transaction we have traced,” he nodded to the portfolio, “every ‘messy translation,’ flows from this clause. It is not a discrepancy. It is a design feature.”

Verhoeven looked at the page. For the first time, her silence was not administrative, but personal. She was reading it. The winter in her eyes deepened. She did not touch it.

“Where,” she asked quietly, “did you obtain this?”

“It was in my father’s satchel,” Léopold said. “The one recovered from the river.”

A lie, seamless and administrative. It placed the document within a known, tragic event. It made it evidence from a closed case.

Verhoeven leaned back. The leather of her chair sighed. She was no longer looking at them, but at the portrait of the King. “A single page. A copy. You understand its existence, in this form, proves nothing. It could be a draft. A theoretical exercise by your father. A forgery, even. The *Société* you refer to was dissolved decades ago. Its assets were folded into the national trust. The paper you hold is a ghost. It has no administrative weight.”

“Then we are in agreement,” Elara said. Her voice had changed. It was no longer cool, but carried the sharp residue of grief Verhoeven had earlier diagnosed. “It is a ghost. And we are here to ask the Ministry of Cultural Harmonization to help us lay it to rest. To officially, and finally, archive this document as a historical curiosity. To issue a statement that it holds no contemporary legal or financial relevance. To reconcile it.”

Léopold understood. This was the purified test. They were not accusing. They were surrendering. They were asking the system to do what it did best: file, categorize, and bury. They were playing the ultimate supplicants, trusting the machine to cover its own tracks. It was the only move it could not refuse without revealing its own hand.

Verhoeven’s gaze returned to them. It was calculating now, stripping them of their roles, seeing the intent beneath. She saw they were not naive grievers. They were auditors. And they had just presented her with an invoice. The cost of filing this ghost was the implicit, permanent silence on the twenty million francs, on the Benga Valley, on all the other ghosts in their portfolio. It was a bribe paid in absence.

A long, silent transaction took place in that sterile room. The hum of the air conditioning was the whirr of the microfilm reader. The clock ticked. Verhoeven finally gave a single, almost imperceptible nod.

“The Ministry,” she said, “exists to bring clarity. To harmonize. This document… this fragment… clearly causes you distress. A distress that clouds your judgment, that leads you to weave narratives. The kindest, clearest action is to remove the source of the confusion.” She stood, a signal the audience was over. She picked up the single page by its corner, as if it were soiled. “I will have it entered into the historical miscellany collection. It will be given a reference number. And that will be the end of it. You have my word.”

Her word. The ultimate currency of the administrative world. Worthless, and binding.

“Your portfolios,” she said, her eyes on the thick folders still in their laps. “You may leave those with me. For contextual review. To ensure the… harmonization… is complete.”

It was the final demand. All their work. Every thread. To be swallowed by the machine, digested into nothingness.

Elara’s hand tightened on her portfolio. Léopold felt the weight of his, the hard evidence of the mechanism. This was the price of safety. The cost of Kofi’s receipt. He thought of his father, writing in the grey Brussels light, building a theory of the cage. Theory was not enough. You had to be willing to enter the cage, and to leave empty-handed, for the door to click shut behind you.

He placed his portfolio on the desk. After a heartbeat, Elara did the same.

Verhoeven allowed herself a small, professional smile. “A wise decision. You have chosen reconciliation. You may go now. The process will be seen to.”

They were dismissed. They rose, turned, and walked out. The door to the waiting room closed behind them with a soft, final click. The terrazzo hallway stretched before them, empty. They walked to the elevator, rode down in the shuddering cage, crossed the vast, echoing lobby. The young man at the desk did not look up. They pushed through the heavy brass doors and emerged into the hot, wet afternoon.

The rain had stopped. The pavement steamed. They walked for two blocks, not touching, not speaking, moving through the crowd of clerks and messengers released for lunch. The ordinary world, breathing the climate.

In a small, shaded square, by a dry fountain, Léopold stopped. He felt hollowed out. Purged. They had given it all away. The evidence, the leverage, the ghost. They had submitted, and been processed.

Elara turned to him. Her severe expression had melted away, leaving something raw and fierce. She reached into the inside pocket of her dress and withdrew a single, folded sheet of paper. She handed it to him.

It was a carbon copy. A list of names, account numbers, and the definitive, triangulated link between the defunct *Société* and a private bank in Zurich. It was the master thread. The one she had not placed in the portfolio. The one she had kept next to her skin.

“The originals are gone,” she said, her voice low. “But a good auditor always keeps a duplicate. Not for the file. For the reckoning.”

Léopold looked at the list. The names were of men now retired, living in quiet villas in Flanders or on sun-drenched coasts. Pillars of a society that believed itself benevolent. The master thread.

“Verhoeven will check the portfolios,” he said. “She will find them thorough, but incomplete. She will believe she has won. That we are neutralized.”

“Yes.”
“And this?”
“This,” Elara said, taking the paper back and folding it away, “is not for the ministry. This is for the newspaper. The opposition party. The magistrate whose brother was passed over for a promotion. We do not fight the machine with a bigger machine. We introduce a virus. A single, irrefutable fact into the wrong bloodstream. We let the immune system attack itself.”

It was the final confrontation, and they had won it by appearing to lose utterly. They had defeated the lingering threat not with a shout, but with a silence, and a single sheet of carbon paper. The threat had been their own sentiment, their desire for a dramatic unveiling. They had sacrificed that. They had let the system file their truth as fiction, and in doing so, had freed the sharper, smaller truth to do its work.

Léopold breathed in the steamy air. The smell of curry from a nearby stall, of wet earth, of diesel. It was the smell of the world, un-administered. He was no longer his father’s son, or Kofi’s friend. He was not even an ally. He was a vector. A carrier. His satchel was empty, but his mind was not. He held the map of the machine’s mind. And now he had the coordinates for its heart.

He was cleansed of the need for vindication. Reborn into the pure, grey work of the audit. It was administrative. It was patient. It was the drip that wears away stone.

“Where do we begin?” he asked.

Elara looked towards the main boulevard, where the traffic flowed like a sluggish river. “We begin with a telephone call. To a man who owes my father a favour. A man who believes in ghosts.”

They walked into the crowd, two more anonymous figures. The hook was no longer in the mystery, nor even in the mechanism. It was in the metastasis. They had planted the seed. Now they would watch for the cracks in the whitewashed walls, for the first, faint tremor in the immaculate desk. Somewhere, in a quiet villa, a telephone would ring. And a retired man, listening to the rain fall on his manicured garden, would feel a chill, as if a door he had locked forty years ago had just, very softly, clicked open.

The real work was not uncovering. It was disseminating. The dossier was compiled. Now it was time to leak.



---

# Chapter 10

## **CHAPTER 10**

The rain in London was a different species. It was not the administrative mist of Brussels, but a heavy, imperial relic, a dowager’s tears falling on the grimy inheritance of a diminished estate. It fell on the soot-stained brick of the terraced house in Camden with a sound like distant applause. Léopold stood on the pavement, his key cold in his hand. The satchel over his shoulder held a change of clothes, a toothbrush, and the clean, sterile weight of his new purpose. The manuscript was gone, metabolized into the system. This was the aftermath.

He let himself in. The air inside was stale and close, carrying the ghost of his old life: dust, the faint chemical tang of cheap printer ink, the underlying sweetness of bananas gone soft in the fruit bowl. It was the smell of suspended animation. His flat had been waiting for him, patient as a tomb. The red light on his answering machine blinked with a slow, metronomic insistence. One. Two. Three. Fourteen messages. The machine was an artifact, a plastic brick from another century. He did not press play. The voices trapped in there belonged to a man he no longer was—editors with querulous deadlines, a cousin in Lyon with news of a birth, the automated reminder of a dental appointment he had missed. They were administrative pings from a closed file.

He moved through the rooms. They seemed smaller, a diorama of a previous self. The desk was a landscape of minor archaeology: coffee rings like tidal marks, stacks of journals, a dried-out pen. He had left here as a man hunting a ghost in a archive. He returned as the ghost himself, tasked with haunting the machine. He opened a window. The damp air rushed in, carrying the scent of wet pavement and fried food from the kebab shop below. The world, un-administered. It was no longer a concept. It was a smell.

He emptied the satchel onto his bed. From between folded shirts, he retrieved the only new artifact: a small, black address book, its pages blank. Elara had given it to him at the Gare du Midi. “For the numbers that cannot be written down,” she had said. “You will know them. Start with the one you already have.” He did. He took a pencil and, in the centre of the first page, wrote a single sequence. It was not a telephone number. It was an internal extension, a routing code for a switchboard in a building that did not officially exist. The man who believed in ghosts.

This was the elixir. Not a trophy, not a decoded secret. It was access. It was the sterile, surgical tool. He looked at his ordinary world—the unpaid bills on the mantelpiece, the fading philodendron on the windowsill—and understood the wound that needed healing. It was not a physical lack. It was the quiet, corrosive acceptance of irrelevance. It was the belief that history was a settled ledger, cosigned by the victors and notarised by time. His journey’s value was to prove the ledger was a forgery, and that the ink was still wet.

He picked up the telephone, its cord coiled like a tired serpent. He dialled the international operator, requested a connection to Accra, and recited the number Kofi had given him a lifetime ago, when they were just two boys with a shared, unspoken weight. He listened to the clicks and whirs as the call was routed through the decaying infrastructure of empires, a path of least resistance through abandoned switchboards and submarine cables.

It was answered on the third ring. “Agyeman.”

The voice was older, wearier than he remembered, sanded down by years of bureaucratic compromise. But it was him.

“Kofi. It’s Léopold.”

A pause. It was not a silence of confusion, but of recalibration. The sound of a man adjusting the focus on a memory he had filed away. “Léopold. Your name has been… circulating. In certain memos that are not memos.”

“I have a number,” Léopold said. His voice was calm, administrative. “An extension. For a man in Geneva. A retired auditor. He is expecting a call about a discrepancy. A ghost in the ledgers from 1959 to 1963. The Katanga files.”

He could hear Kofi’s breath, a soft hiss on the line. The Katanga files. The words were not a revelation, but a key turning in a lock. They referred to the mineral audits from the secession, a maze of shell companies and redirected shipments whose paper trail had been declared lost in a fire. Kofi had spent two years of his early career trying to reconstruct them, before being quietly reassigned to agricultural subsidies.

“That is a dead end,” Kofi said, but his tone was not dismissive. It was cautious. “Officially, it does not exist.”

“Officially,” Léopold echoed. “But the ghost does. The man in Geneva has the parallel books. The ones they kept to audit the auditors. He needs a corroborating source. A name from the ground, then. Someone who saw the trucks that were not on the manifests.”

He was not asking for a favour. He was offering a suture. He was using the reward—the map, the access—to heal a specific, old wound in Kofi’s world: the professional humiliation of a truth deliberately obscured, a career diverted into harmless channels. This was the value. Not vindication, but correction. A single, irrefutable fact placed in the right bloodstream.

The pause this time was longer. Léopold heard the distant sound of Accra traffic, a horn blaring, a life utterly disconnected from this grey London room and the spectral geography of colonial finance.

“There is an old man,” Kofi said finally, his voice dropping into a lower, more concrete register. “In Lubumbashi. He was a clerk for Union Minière. He kept his own notebooks. For his own conscience. He is poor. He is afraid. But he is tired of being afraid. I have his address. It is not written down.”

“Tell me,” Léopold said. He picked up the pencil.

*

The recognition did not come with ceremony. It came with a quiet, seismic shift in the atmosphere of his own life. Two days later, Léopold visited the reading room of the British Library. It was a test. A return to the scene of the original crime, where his father’s ghost had first pressed the hook into his hand.

He ordered a file—the personal papers of a minor colonial functionary from the Belgian Congo, a man whose diaries were deemed of no historical consequence. The request was processed without comment. But when the grey box was placed before him, the young archivist, a woman with severe glasses and a placid demeanor, did not immediately retreat.

She leaned in, her voice a library whisper that carried an unusual charge. “You’ll find the foliation in the third volume is inconsistent with the catalogue entry. My predecessor noted the discrepancy, but it was never corrected.” She met his eye for a fraction of a second too long. “A researcher last year spent weeks trying to reconcile it. He found it most frustrating.”

Then she was gone, gliding back to her desk.

Léopold opened the box. The archivist’s comment was not professional courtesy. It was a signal. The “frustrated researcher” was a man named Arnaud, a footnote in his own investigation, a scholar who had been quietly barred from several European archives after asking the wrong questions. By mentioning him, she was placing Léopold on a map. She was recognising him as part of a community—not of allies, but of vectors. A silent, scattered fellowship engaged in the pure, grey work of the audit. His change was not celebrated; it was acknowledged in the only currency that mattered here: a warning about foliation.

He spent the afternoon not reading the diaries, but comparing the physical evidence of the file itself. The misplaced pages, the subtle change in paper stock for a six-month period in 1957. It was administrative. It was patient. It was the drip.

On his way out, he stopped at the desk. The archivist looked up.

“Thank you,” he said. “The foliation was indeed instructive.”

She gave a small, precise nod. “It often is. Good day, Mr. Verhoeven.”

He had not given her his name.

*

The reconciliation with figures from his old life was not a meeting. It was an obituary. That evening, the red light on his machine still blinking its futile rhythm, Léopold opened the *Financial Times*. He skimmed the dense thicket of figures and analyses, the daily liturgy of the machine. His eye caught a small headline in the World News section, bottom of page six: **Belgian Industrialist and Philanthropist Dies at 78**.

It was a quarter-column. It listed achievements, board memberships, charitable foundations. It mentioned his late wife, his surviving children. It noted he had been a keen yachtsman. The prose was smooth, lacquered, final. It was the official story, administrated to the press by a efficient family office. There was no mention of a son named Léopold. The wound of that omission was an old one, but he felt it now as a clean scar. The man who had been his father was gone. The archive that had haunted him was now just… archive. Neutralised. Filed.

He felt no triumph, only the hollow clarity of a debt erased. The hook was gone, extracted not by understanding, but by rendering the mystery obsolete. He was not his father’s son. The statement was now a simple fact, not a rebellion. He closed the paper. The rain tapped against the window. Somewhere, in a quiet villa in Geneva, a retired man would be reading the same notice, perhaps with a different, colder eye. A ghost, recognising the passing of another ghost.

*
